\begin{example}[Half-open rectangles]\label{ex:half-open-rectangles} % assignment 3, problem 5
Let $d \in \N$. 
The half-open rectangles
\[
\mathcal{C} 
\coloneq
\{(\vect{a}, \vect{b}] : \vect{a},\vect{b} \in \R^d, \vect{a} \leq \vect{b}\}
\]
form a semiring:
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item $\varnothing = (\vect{a}, \vect{a}] \in \mathcal{C}$. \cmark

\item Let $A = (\vect{a},\vect{b}], B = (\vect{c},\vect{d}] \in \mathcal{C}$.
Then\tikzmark{intersection-rectangles}
\[
A \cap B
=
\begin{cases}
\varnothing & \text{if $a_i \geq d_i$ or $c_i \geq b_i$ for some $i = 1,\dots,d$} \\
(\max(\vect{a},\vect{c}), \min(\vect{b},\vect{d})] & \text{otherwise}
\end{cases}
\]%
\tikzexternaldisable%
\begin{tikzpicture}[remember picture,overlay]%
\coordinate (IntersectionRectanglesCoord) at (pic cs:intersection-rectangles);

% figure parameters
\def\scaleFactor{0.25}

% A = (a,b],  B = (c,d]
\def\xA{0.0}
\def\yA{0.0}
\def\wA{4.0}
\def\hA{3.0}

\def\xB{3.0}
\def\yB{1.2}
\def\wB{3.6}
\def\hB{3.4}

\begin{scope}[
  shift={([xshift=-0.0em,yshift=-0.0em]current page text area.east|-IntersectionRectanglesCoord)}
]
\begin{scope}[
  xshift=-2.5cm,
  yshift=0.25cm,
  scale=\scaleFactor,
  line cap=round,
  line join=round,
  font=\scriptsize,
  every node/.style={font=\scriptsize}
]


% \fill[green!80, fill opacity=0.1, even odd rule] 
%   (\xA,\yA) rectangle (\xA+\wA,\yA+\hA)
%   (\xB,\yB) rectangle (\xB+\wB,\yB+\hB);
\fill[green!80, fill opacity=0.1]
  (\xB,\yB) rectangle (\xA+\wA,\yA+\hA);

% rectangle A = (a,b]
% open sides: left, bottom dashed
% closed sides: top, right solid
\draw[densely dotted, ] (\xA,\yA) -- (\xA,\yA+\hA);
\draw[densely dotted, ] (\xA,\yA) -- (\xA+\wA,\yA);
\draw[] (\xA,\yA+\hA) -- (\xA+\wA,\yA+\hA);
\draw[] (\xA+\wA,\yA) -- (\xA+\wA,\yA+\hA);

% rectangle B = (c,d]
\draw[densely dotted, ] (\xB,\yB) -- (\xB,\yB+\hB);
\draw[densely dotted, ] (\xB,\yB) -- (\xB+\wB,\yB);
\draw[] (\xB,\yB+\hB) -- (\xB+\wB,\yB+\hB);
\draw[] (\xB+\wB,\yB) -- (\xB+\wB,\yB+\hB);

% labels
\node at (\xA+1.55,\yA+1.15) {$A$};
\node at (\xB+2.00,\yB+1.75) {$B$};

\node[anchor=east, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=1pt]  at (\xA,\yA+0.50*\hA) {$a_1$};
\node[anchor=north, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=1pt] at (\xA+0.50*\wA,\yA) {$a_2$};

\node[anchor=south, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=1pt] at (\xA+0.20*\wA,\yA+\hA) {$b_2$};
\node[anchor=west, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=1pt]  at (\xA+\wA,\yA+0.1*\hA) {$b_1$};

\node[anchor=east, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=1pt] at (\xB,\yB+0.8*\hB) {$c_1$};
\node[anchor=north, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=1pt]      at (\xB+0.80*\wB,\yB) {$c_2$};

\node[anchor=south, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=1pt] at (\xB+0.50*\wB,\yB+\hB) {$d_2$};
\node[anchor=west, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=1pt]  at (\xB+\wB,\yB+0.50*\hB) {$d_1$};

\end{scope}
\end{scope}
\end{tikzpicture}%
\tikzexternalenable%
where $\max$ and $\min$ are taken componentwise.
In both cases, $A \cap B \in \mathcal{C}$. \cmark

\item Let $A = (\vect{a},\vect{b}]$ and $B = (\vect{\alpha},\vect{\beta}]$ be in $\mathcal{C}$ with $B \subseteq A$.
For every dimension $i = 1,\dots,d$, define three intervals
\[
I_i(L) = (a_i,\alpha_i] 
\qquad
I_i(M) = (\alpha_i,\beta_i]
\qquad
I_i(R) = (\beta_i,b_i]
\]
where we use the convention that $I_i( \cdot ) = \varnothing$ if the left endpoint is equal to the right endpoint (Since $B \subseteq A$, it cannot be greater than the right endpoint).
Every vector $\vect{s} = [s_1,\dots,s_d] \in \{L,M,R\}^d$ corresponds to a rectangle 
\[
C(\vect{s}) \coloneq \bigtimes_{i=1}^d I_i(s_i)
\]
which is in $\mathcal{C}$.
Note that rectangles corresponding to different vectors are disjoint.
Furthermore, the rectangle corresponding to $\vect{s} = [M,\dots,M]$ is exactly $B$, i.e. $C([M,\dots,M]) = B$
and
the union of all rectangles corresponding to all possible $3^d$ vectors $\vect{s} \in \{L,M,R\}^d$ is exactly $A$, i.e. $\bigcup_{\vect{s} \in \{L,M,R\}^d} C(\vect{s}) = A$.
Therefore, we can write $A \setminus B$ as\tikzmark{setminus-rectangles}
\[
A \setminus B
=
\bigcup_{\substack{\vect{s} \in \{L,M,R\}^d\\ \vect{s} \neq [M,\dots,M]}} C(\vect{s})
\]%
\tikzexternaldisable%
\begin{tikzpicture}[remember picture,overlay]%
\coordinate (SetminusRectanglesCoord) at (pic cs:setminus-rectangles);

% figure parameters
\def\scaleFactor{0.25}

% outer rectangle A = (a,b]
\def\xA{0.0}
\def\yA{0.0}
\def\wA{8.6}
\def\hA{4.9}

% inner rectangle B = (alpha,beta]
\def\xB{2.25}
\def\yB{1.15}
\def\wB{2.25}
\def\hB{2.60}

\pgfmathsetmacro{\xR}{\xB+\wB}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\yT}{\yB+\hB}

\begin{scope}[
  shift={([xshift=2cm,yshift=-4.4em]current page text area.east|-SetminusRectanglesCoord)}
]
\begin{scope}[
  xshift=-4.7cm,
  yshift=0cm,
  scale=\scaleFactor,
  line cap=round,
  line join=round,
  font=\scriptsize,
  every node/.style={font=\scriptsize}
]

% A = (a,b]
\draw[densely dotted, ] (\xA,\yA) -- (\xA,\yA+\hA);
\draw[densely dotted, ] (\xA,\yA) -- (\xA+\wA,\yA);
\draw[] (\xA,\yA+\hA) -- (\xA+\wA,\yA+\hA);
\draw[] (\xA+\wA,\yA) -- (\xA+\wA,\yA+\hA);

% partition lines through B (orange)
\draw[orange!90!black, ]
  (\xB,\yA) -- (\xB,\yB)
  (\xB,\yT) -- (\xB,\yA+\hA);

\draw[orange!90!black, ]
  (\xR,\yA) -- (\xR,\yB)
  (\xR,\yT) -- (\xR,\yA+\hA);

\draw[orange!90!black, ]
  (\xA,\yB) -- (\xB,\yB)
  (\xR,\yB) -- (\xA+\wA,\yB);

\draw[orange!90!black, ]
  (\xA,\yT) -- (\xB,\yT)
  (\xR,\yT) -- (\xA+\wA,\yT);

% B = (alpha,beta]
\fill[purple!70, opacity=0.45] (\xB,\yB) rectangle (\xR,\yT);
\draw[densely dotted, ] (\xB,\yB) -- (\xB,\yT);
\draw[densely dotted, ] (\xB,\yB) -- (\xR,\yB);
\draw[] (\xB,\yT) -- (\xR,\yT);
\draw[] (\xR,\yB) -- (\xR,\yT);

% labels
\node[font=\footnotesize, fill=white, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=0.0pt] at (\xA+6.9,\yA+3.7) {$A$};
\node[font=\footnotesize] at (\xB+0.5*\wB,\yB+0.5*\hB) {$B$};

\node[font=\tiny,anchor=east, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=0.5pt]  at (\xA,\yA+0.5*\hA) {$a_1$};
\node[font=\tiny,anchor=north, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=0.5pt] at (\xA+0.5*\wA,\yA) {$a_2$};

\node[font=\tiny,anchor=west, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=0.5pt]  at (\xA+\wA,\yA+0.5*\hA) {$b_1$};
\node[font=\tiny,anchor=south, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=0.5pt] at (\xA+0.5*\wA,\yA+\hA) {$b_2$};

\node[font=\tiny,anchor=east, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=0.5pt]  at (\xB,\yB+0.5*\hB) {$\alpha_1$};
\node[font=\tiny,anchor=north, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=0.5pt] at (\xB+0.5*\wB,\yB) {$\alpha_2$};

\node[font=\tiny,anchor=west, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=0.5pt]  at (\xR,\yB+0.5*\hB) {$\beta_1$};
\node[font=\tiny,anchor=south, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=0.5pt] at (\xB+0.5*\wB,\yT) {$\beta_2$};

% dimension annotations
\draw[very thin, {Classical TikZ Rightarrow[length=1.0pt, width=2pt]}-{Classical TikZ Rightarrow[length=1.0pt, width=2pt]}] (\xA,-0.9) -- node[fill=white, font=\tiny, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=0pt] {$L$} (\xB,-0.9);
\draw[very thin, {Classical TikZ Rightarrow[length=1.0pt, width=2pt]}-{Classical TikZ Rightarrow[length=1.0pt, width=2pt]}] (\xB,-0.9) -- node[fill=white, font=\tiny, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=0pt] {$M$} (\xR,-0.9);
\draw[very thin, {Classical TikZ Rightarrow[length=1.0pt, width=2pt]}-{Classical TikZ Rightarrow[length=1.0pt, width=2pt]}] (\xR,-0.9) -- node[fill=white, font=\tiny, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=0pt] {$R$} (\xA+\wA,-0.9);

\draw[very thin, {Classical TikZ Rightarrow[length=1.0pt, width=2pt]}-{Classical TikZ Rightarrow[length=1.0pt, width=2pt]}] (-1.75,\yA) -- node[fill=white, font=\tiny, inner sep=0.1pt, outer sep=0pt] {$L$} (-1.75,\yB);
\draw[very thin, {Classical TikZ Rightarrow[length=1.0pt, width=2pt]}-{Classical TikZ Rightarrow[length=1.0pt, width=2pt]}] (-1.75,\yB) -- node[fill=white, font=\tiny, inner sep=0.1pt, outer sep=0pt] {$M$} (-1.75,\yT);
\draw[very thin, {Classical TikZ Rightarrow[length=1.0pt, width=2pt]}-{Classical TikZ Rightarrow[length=1.0pt, width=2pt]}] (-1.75,\yT) -- node[fill=white, font=\tiny, inner sep=0.1pt, outer sep=0pt] {$R$} (-1.75,\yA+\hA);

\end{scope}
\end{scope}
\end{tikzpicture}%
\tikzexternalenable%
where $\vect{s} = [s_1,\dots,s_d]$ and $s_i \in \{L,M,R\}$ for $i = 1,\dots,d$.
The union is over (at most) $3^d - 1$ sets, hence finite. \cmark
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{example}



Similarly to measures (see \autoref{def:measure}), we define the following properties of set functions on semirings
\begin{definition} \label{def:set-function-properties}
Let $\mathcal{S} \subseteq 2^\Omega$ be a semiring and $\mu : \mathcal{S} \to [0,\infty]$ be a set function.
We say that $\mu$ is
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item \label{def:set-function-additive}
\hl[2]{\emph{additive}} if for any $A_1, \ldots, A_n \in \mathcal{S}$ with $A_i \cap A_j = \varnothing$ for $i \neq j$ and $\bigcup_{i=1}^n A_i \in \mathcal{S}$, there holds
\[
\mu\left(\bigcup_{i=1}^n A_i\right) = \sum_{i=1}^n \mu(A_i)
\]
\item \label{def:set-function-sigma-subadditive}
\hl[2]{\emph{$\sigma$-subadditive}} if for any $A \in \mathcal{S}$ and any $A_1, A_2, \ldots \in \mathcal{S}$ with $A \subseteq \bigcup_{i=1}^\infty A_i$ 
(i.e. they cover $A$)%
, there holds
\[
\mu(A) \leq \sum_{i=1}^\infty \mu(A_i)
\]
\item \label{def:set-function-sigma-finite}
\hl[2]{\emph{$\sigma$-finite}} if there exists a sequence $A_1, A_2, \ldots \in \mathcal{S}$ such that $\Omega = \bigcup_{i=1}^\infty A_i$ and $\mu(A_i) < \infty$ for all $i \in \N$.
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{definition}

On a semiring, additivity is sufficient to guarantee finite subadditivity:
\begin{lemma}\label{lem:on-semiring-additivity-implies-finite-subadditivity}
Let $\mathcal{S} \subseteq 2^\Omega$ be a semiring and $\mu : \mathcal{S} \to [0,\infty]$ be an additive set function.
Then $\mu$ is also finitely subadditive, i.e. for any $A \in \mathcal{S}$ and any $A_1, \ldots, A_n \in \mathcal{S}$ with
$A \subseteq \bigcup_{i=1}^n A_i$, 
there holds
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
\mu(A) \leq \sum_{i=1}^n \mu(A_i)
\end{verticalhack}
\qedhere
\]
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Define
\[
B_k := A_k \setminus \bigcup_{i=1}^{k-1} A_i
=
\bigcap_{i=1}^{k-1}\bigl(A_k \setminus (A_k \cap A_i)\bigr)
\]
for $k = 1,\dots,n$,
where we use the convention that $\bigcup_{i=1}^0 A_i = \varnothing$.
This is the standard disjointification of $A_1,\dots,A_n$, i.e.
\[
\bigsqcup_{k=1}^n B_k = \bigcup_{k=1}^n A_k
\]
and $B_{k_1} \cap B_{k_2} = \varnothing$ for $k_1 \neq k_2$, which is emphasized by the symbol `$\bigsqcup$'.

For every \(k=1,\dots,n\) and every \(i=1,\dots,k-1\), we have
\(
A_k \cap A_i \in \mathcal{S}
\)
by \autoref{def:semiring} \ref{def:semiring-closure-intersection}.
Hence, by \autoref{def:semiring} \ref{def:semiring-subset-difference-disjoint-union}, each set
\(
A_k \setminus (A_k \cap A_i)
\)
is a finite disjoint union of sets in \(\mathcal{S}\).
Therefore, \(B_k\), being a finite intersection of such sets, is a finite union of sets in \(\mathcal{S}\).
Refining this finite union if necessary, there exist \(c_k \in \N\) and pairwise disjoint sets
\(
C_{k,1},\dots,C_{k,c_k} \in \mathcal{S}
\)
such that
\[
B_k = \bigsqcup_{i=1}^{c_k} C_{k,i}
\]
% For \(k=1\), we simply take \(c_1=1\) and \(C_{1,1}=A_1\).

Now consider
\[
A_k \setminus B_k
=
A_k \cap \bigcup_{i=1}^{k-1} A_i
=
\bigcup_{i=1}^{k-1} (A_k \cap A_i)
\]
Since each \(A_k \cap A_i\) belongs to \(\mathcal{S}\), the set \(A_k \setminus B_k\) is a finite union of sets in \(\mathcal{S}\).
Refining again if necessary, there exist \(d_k \in \N\) and pairwise disjoint sets
\(
D_{k,1},\dots,D_{k,d_k} \in \mathcal{S}
\)
such that
\[
A_k \setminus B_k = \bigsqcup_{i=1}^{d_k} D_{k,i}
\]

Since
\[
A_k
=
B_k \sqcup (A_k \setminus B_k)
=
\left(\bigsqcup_{i=1}^{c_k} C_{k,i}\right)
\sqcup
\left(\bigsqcup_{i=1}^{d_k} D_{k,i}\right)
\]
is a disjoint union, additivity yields
\[
\mu(A_k)
=
\sum_{i=1}^{c_k} \mu(C_{k,i}) + \sum_{i=1}^{d_k} \mu(D_{k,i})
\geq
\sum_{i=1}^{c_k} \mu(C_{k,i})
\]

Moreover, the sets \(B_1,\dots,B_n\) are pairwise disjoint and
\begin{equation}\label{eq:A-subset-union-Cki}
A \subseteq \bigcup_{k=1}^n A_k 
= 
\bigsqcup_{k=1}^n B_k
=
\bigsqcup_{k=1}^n \bigsqcup_{i=1}^{c_k} C_{k,i}
\end{equation}
Hence
\[
A
=
\bigsqcup_{k=1}^n \bigsqcup_{i=1}^{c_k} (C_{k,i} \cap A)
\]
where the union is disjoint.
By additivity, we have
\[
\mu(A)
= % why?
\sum_{k=1}^n \sum_{i=1}^{c_k} \mu(C_{k,i} \cap A)
\leq % why?
\sum_{k=1}^n \sum_{i=1}^{c_k} \mu(C_{k,i})
\leq % why?
\sum_{k=1}^n \mu(A_k)
\]
Hence \(\mu\) is finitely subadditive.
\end{proof}

\pagebreak[2]

\begin{theorem}[\textcolor{red}{Carathéodory Extension}]\label{thm:caratheodory-extension}
Let $\mathcal{S} \subseteq 2^\Omega$ be a semiring and 
\[
\mu : \mathcal{S} \to [0,\infty]
\]
be an additive \ref{def:set-function-additive}, $\sigma$-subadditive \ref{def:set-function-sigma-subadditive} and $\sigma$-finite \ref{def:set-function-sigma-finite} set function with $\mu(\varnothing) = 0$.
Then there is a unique $\sigma$-finite measure % (\autoref{def:measure})
\[
\tilde{\mu} : \sigma(\mathcal{S}) \to [0,\infty]
\]
such that $\tilde{\mu}(E) = \mu(E)$ for all $E \in \mathcal{S}$.
\end{theorem}

Using \autoref{thm:caratheodory-extension}, we are now able to prove the following result:
\begin{theorem}\label{thm:lebesgue-borel-measure}
There exists a uniquely determined measure $\lambda^d$ on $(\R^d, \mathcal{B}(\R^d))$ with the property that
\[
\lambda^d((\vect{a}, \vect{b}]) 
=
\prod_{i=1}^d (b_i - a_i)
\]
for all $\vect{a},\vect{b} \in \R^d$ with $\vect{a} < \vect{b}$.
The measure $\lambda^d$ is called the \emph{Lebesgue-Borel measure}.
\end{theorem}

\begin{definition}
% [Bounded]
\label{def:bounded-set}
A set $K \subseteq \R^d$ is called \emph{bounded} if there exist $\vect{x}\in\R^d$ and $r>0$ such that
\[
K \subseteq B_r(\vect{x})
\]
where $B_r(\vect{x})$ is the open ball defined in \autoref{ex:canonical-topology}.
\end{definition}
\begin{definition}
An \emph{open cover} of a set $K \subseteq \R^d$ is a family $(O_i)_{i\in I}$ with $O_i \in \mathcal{T}$\footnote{$\mathcal{T}$ denotes the canonical topology on $\R^d$, see \autoref{ex:canonical-topology}} for all $i\in I$ such that
\[
K \subseteq \bigcup_{i\in I} O_i
\]
A \emph{finite subcover} is a finite subfamily \(O_{i_1},\dots,O_{i_n}\) with
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
K \subseteq \bigcup_{k=1}^n O_{i_k}
\end{verticalhack}
\qedhere
\]
\end{definition}
\begin{remark}
In $\R^d$, the following definitions of compact sets are equivalent:
\begin{itemize}
\item $K$ is closed (\autoref{def:topology}) and bounded (\autoref{def:bounded-set})
\item every open cover of $K$ admits a finite subcover 
\end{itemize}
This is the Heine--Borel theorem.
\end{remark}


\begin{proof}[of \autoref{thm:lebesgue-borel-measure}]
Let
\(
\mathcal{C}
\)
be the semiring of half-open rectangles from \autoref{ex:half-open-rectangles}, and define
\[
\mu\bigl((\vect{a},\vect{b}]\bigr)
:=
\prod_{j=1}^d (b_j-a_j)
\]
This set function \(\mu\) is additive, since the volume of a box equals the sum of the volumes of finitely many disjoint boxes forming it.
Hence, in order to apply \autoref{thm:caratheodory-extension}, it remains to verify that \(\mu\) is \(\sigma\)-subadditive and \(\sigma\)-finite.

We first show \(\sigma\)-subadditivity. Let
\(
(\vect{a},\vect{b}],\ (\vect{a}^{(1)},\vect{b}^{(1)}],\ (\vect{a}^{(2)},\vect{b}^{(2)}],\dots \in \mathcal{C}
\)
with
\[
(\vect{a},\vect{b}] \subseteq \bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} (\vect{a}^{(i)},\vect{b}^{(i)}]
\]
We show that
\begin{equation}
\mu\bigl((\vect{a},\vect{b}]\bigr)
\le
\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} \mu\bigl((\vect{a}^{(i)},\vect{b}^{(i)}]\bigr)
\label{eq:mu-sigma-subadditivity-rectangles}
\end{equation}

To this end, we use a compactness argument to reduce \eqref{eq:mu-sigma-subadditivity-rectangles} to finite subadditivity.
Let \(\varepsilon>0\). For every \(i\in\N\), choose
\(
\vect{b}^{(i)}_\varepsilon > \vect{b}^{(i)}
\)
(componentwise) such that
\begin{equation}\label{eq:lebesgue-borel-choose-b-epsilon}
\mu\bigl((\vect{a}^{(i)},\vect{b}^{(i)}_\varepsilon]\bigr)
\le
\mu\bigl((\vect{a}^{(i)},\vect{b}^{(i)}]\bigr) + \varepsilon 2^{-i-1}
\end{equation}
Further choose \(\vect{a}_\varepsilon \in (\vect{a},\vect{b})\) such that
\begin{equation}\label{eq:lebesgue-borel-choose-a-epsilon}
\mu\bigl((\vect{a}_\varepsilon,\vect{b}]\bigr)
\ge
\mu\bigl((\vect{a},\vect{b}]\bigr) - \frac{\varepsilon}{2}
\end{equation}

Then we have the inclusions
\[
[\vect{a}_\varepsilon,\vect{b}]
\subseteq
(\vect{a},\vect{b}]
\subseteq
\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} (\vect{a}^{(i)},\vect{b}^{(i)}]
\subseteq
\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} (\vect{a}^{(i)},\vect{b}^{(i)}_\varepsilon)
\]
% Indeed, the last union is now a union of open rectangles, hence an open cover of the compact set \([\vect{a}_\varepsilon,\vect{b}]\).
% Since \([\vect{a}_\varepsilon,\vect{b}]\) is compact, there exists \(N\in\N\) such that
Since \([\vect{a}_\varepsilon,\vect{b}]\) is compact and the last union is an open cover of it, there exists \(N\in\N\) such that
\[
(\vect{a}_\varepsilon,\vect{b}]
\subseteq
[\vect{a}_\varepsilon,\vect{b}]
\subseteq
\bigcup_{i=1}^{N} (\vect{a}^{(i)},\vect{b}^{(i)}_\varepsilon)
\subseteq
\bigcup_{i=1}^{N} (\vect{a}^{(i)},\vect{b}^{(i)}_\varepsilon]
\]


Since \(\mu\) is finitely subadditive on \(\mathcal{C}\) (see \autoref{lem:on-semiring-additivity-implies-finite-subadditivity}), we obtain
\begin{equation}\label{eq:lebesgue-borel-finite-subadditivity}
\mu\bigl((\vect{a}_\varepsilon,\vect{b}]\bigr)
\le
\sum_{i=1}^{N} \mu\bigl((\vect{a}^{(i)},\vect{b}^{(i)}_\varepsilon]\bigr)
\end{equation}
Combining \eqref{eq:lebesgue-borel-choose-a-epsilon}, \eqref{eq:lebesgue-borel-finite-subadditivity} and \eqref{eq:lebesgue-borel-choose-b-epsilon}, we conclude
\[
\mu\bigl((\vect{a},\vect{b}]\bigr)
\le
\frac{\varepsilon}{2}
+
\sum_{i=1}^{N}
\left(
\mu\bigl((\vect{a}^{(i)},\vect{b}^{(i)}]\bigr)
+
\varepsilon 2^{-i-1}
\right)
\]
Hence
\[
\mu\bigl((\vect{a},\vect{b}]\bigr)
\le
\frac{\varepsilon}{2}
+
\sum_{i=1}^{N}\mu\bigl((\vect{a}^{(i)},\vect{b}^{(i)}]\bigr)
+
\sum_{i=1}^{N}\varepsilon 2^{-i-1}
\le
\varepsilon
+
\sum_{i=1}^{\infty}\mu\bigl((\vect{a}^{(i)},\vect{b}^{(i)}]\bigr)
\]
since 
% \(\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} 2^{-i-1} = \frac{1}{2}\) and
\(\mu(\cdot) \geq 0\).
Letting \(\varepsilon \searrow  0\) yields \eqref{eq:mu-sigma-subadditivity-rectangles}. Thus \(\mu\) is \(\sigma\)-subadditive.

It remains to check \(\sigma\)-finiteness. 
But
\(
\R^d
=
\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} (-n,n]^d
\)
and
\(
\mu\bigl((-n,n]^d\bigr) = (2n)^d < \infty
\)
for all $n\in\N$.
Hence \(\mu\) is \(\sigma\)-finite.

All assumptions of \autoref{thm:caratheodory-extension} are therefore satisfied. 
Consequently, there exists a unique \(\sigma\)-finite measure
\(
\lambda^d : \mathcal{B}(\R^d)=\sigma(\mathcal{C}) \to [0,\infty]
\)
such that
\(
\lambda^d\bigl((\vect{a},\vect{b}]\bigr)
=
\mu\bigl((\vect{a},\vect{b}]\bigr)
=
\prod_{j=1}^d (b_j-a_j)
\)
for all \(\vect{a},\vect{b}\in\R^d\) with \(\vect{a}<\vect{b}\).
This is the Lebesgue--Borel measure.
\end{proof}









\begin{definition}[Semicontinuity]
A function $f : \R \to \R$ is called 
\begin{itemize}
\item 
\emph{upper semicontinuous} if for every $x_0 \in \R$ and every $\varepsilon > 0$ there exists $\delta > 0$ such that
\[
|x-x_0| < \delta \implies f(x) < f(x_0) + \varepsilon
\]
\item 
\emph{lower semicontinuous} if for every $x_0 \in \R$ and every $\varepsilon > 0$ there exists $\delta > 0$ such that
\[
|x-x_0| < \delta \implies f(x) > f(x_0) - \varepsilon
\]
\end{itemize}
% \begin{figure}[ht]
%   \newcommand{\leftrightcontcolor}{black}
%   \centering
%   \captionsetup[subfigure]{labelformat=empty}
%   \subfloat[upper semicontinuous, but \\not lower semicontinuous at $x_0$]{%
%     \begin{tikzpicture}
%       \begin{axis}[
%         axis x line=bottom,
%         axis y line=left,
%         axis line style={
%     ->,
%       >=Computer Modern Rightarrow
%     },
%         xlabel={$x$},ylabel={$f(x)$},
%         xlabel style={at={(axis description cs:1,0)},anchor=south,yshift=2pt},
%         ylabel style={at={(axis description cs:0.35,1)},anchor=west,xshift=2pt,rotate=-90},
%         width=5.5cm,height=4cm,
%         xmin=-1,xmax=1,ymin=0,ymax=1.4,
%         xtick={0},xticklabels={$x_0$},
%         ytick=\empty
%       ]
%         % data shifted up by 0.2
%         \addplot[thick,\leftrightcontcolor,domain=-1:0]{0.4};
%         \addplot[thick,\leftrightcontcolor,domain=0:1]{0.4};
%         % markers
%         \addplot[only marks,mark=*,thick,\leftrightcontcolor,mark options={fill=white}]
%           coordinates {(0,0.4)};
%         \addplot[only marks,mark=*,thick,\leftrightcontcolor]
%           coordinates {(0,1.0)};
%         % jump
%         \draw[densely dashed,\leftrightcontcolor] (axis cs:0,0.4) -- (axis cs:0,1.0);
%       \end{axis}
%     \end{tikzpicture}
%   }%
%   \hspace{5em}
%   \subfloat[lower semicontinuous, but \\not upper semicontinuous at $x_0$]{%
%     \begin{tikzpicture}
%       \begin{axis}[
%         axis x line=bottom,
%         axis y line=left,
%         axis line style={
%     ->,
%       >=Computer Modern Rightarrow
%     },
%         xlabel={$x$},ylabel={$f(x)$},
%         xlabel style={at={(axis description cs:1,0)},anchor=south,yshift=2pt},
%         ylabel style={at={(axis description cs:0.35,1)},anchor=west,xshift=2pt,rotate=-90},
%         width=5.5cm,height=4cm,
%         xmin=-1,xmax=1,ymin=0,ymax=1.4,
%         xtick={0},xticklabels={$x_0$},
%         ytick=\empty
%       ]
%         % data shifted up by 0.2
%         \addplot[thick,\leftrightcontcolor,domain=-1:0]{1.0};
%         \addplot[thick,\leftrightcontcolor,domain=0:1]{1.0};
%         % markers
%         \addplot[only marks,mark=*,thick,\leftrightcontcolor]
%           coordinates {(0,0.4)};
%         \addplot[only marks,mark=*,thick,\leftrightcontcolor,mark options={fill=white}]
%           coordinates {(0,1.0)};
%         % jump
%         \draw[densely dashed,\leftrightcontcolor] (axis cs:0,1.0) -- (axis cs:0,0.4);
%       \end{axis}
%     \end{tikzpicture}
%   }%
% \end{figure}
A real-valued function $f$ is upper (respectively, lower) semicontinuous at a point $x_0$ if, roughly speaking, the function values for arguments near $x_0$ are not much higher (respectively, lower) than $f\left(x_0\right)$. 
Equivalently, a function on a domain $X$ is lower semi-continuous if its epigraph $\{(x, t) \in X \times \mathbb{R}: t \geq f(x)\}$ is closed in $X \times \R$, and upper semi-continuous if $-f$ is lower semi-continuous.
\end{definition}


\begin{example}[Lebesgue-Stieltjes measure]\label{ex:lebesgue-stieltjes-measure}
Let $\Omega = \R$ and $\mathcal{S} = \{(a, b] : a,b \in \R, a \leq b\}$.
$\mathcal{S}$ is a semiring (see \autoref{ex:half-open-rectangles}) with $\sigma(\mathcal{S}) = \mathcal{B}(\R)$.

Consider a monotonically increasing and right-continuous function $F : \R \to \R$.
Then the set function
\[
\begin{aligned}
\tilde{\mu}_F : \mathcal{S} &\longrightarrow [0,\infty) \\
(a,b] &\longmapsto \tilde{\mu}_F((a,b]) = F(b) - F(a)
\end{aligned}
\]
is 
additive, 
$\sigma$-subadditive,
$\sigma$-finite 
and satisfies $\tilde{\mu}_F(\varnothing) = 0$.
% Moreover,
% \(
% \R = \bigcup_{n=1}^\infty (-n,n]
% \)
% with $(-n,n]\in\mathcal{S}$ and
% \[
% \tilde{\mu}_F((-n,n]) = F(n)-F(-n)<\infty
% \]
% for all $n\in\N$.
% Hence $\tilde{\mu}_F$ is $\sigma$-finite on $\mathcal{S}$.

By \autoref{thm:caratheodory-extension}, we can uniquely extend $\tilde{\mu}_F$ to a $\sigma$-finite measure $\mu_F$ on $\mathcal{B}(\R)$, called the \emph{Lebesgue-Stieltjes measure} with \emph{distribution function} $F$.
\end{example}

\begin{definition}
A right continuous monotonically increasing function $F : \R \to [0, 1]$ with
\[
F(-\infty) \coloneq \lim_{x \to -\infty} F(x) = 0
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
F(\infty) \coloneq \lim_{x \to \infty} F(x) = 1
\]
is called a \emph{cumulative distribution function}, \emph{probability distribution function} or simply \emph{\hl{distribution function}}.
If $\Prob$ is a probability measure on $(\R, \mathcal{B}(\R))$, then
\[
\begin{aligned}
F_{\Prob} : \R &\longrightarrow [0,1] \\
x &\longmapsto F_{\Prob}(x) = \Prob((-\infty,x])
\end{aligned}
\]
is called the \emph{distribution function} of $\Prob$.
\end{definition}




Clearly, $F_{\Prob}$ is right continuous and $F_{\Prob}(-\infty)=0$, since $\Prob$ is upper semicontinuous and finite. 
Since $\Prob$ is lower semicontinuous, we have $F_{\Prob}(\infty)=\Prob(\R)=1$. 
Therefore, $F_{\Prob}$ is indeed a distribution function if $\Prob$ is a probability measure. 
More generally, every finite measure on $(\R, \mathcal{B}(\R))$ is a Lebesgue-Stieltjes measure for some function $F$.

\begin{theorem}
The map $\Prob \longmapsto F_{\Prob}$ is a bijection from the set of probability measures on $(\R, \allowbreak \mathcal{B}(\R))$ to the set of probability distribution functions.
\end{theorem}


\begin{exercise}[Lebesgue-Stieltjes measure for step functions]\label{exer:lebesgue-stieltjes-step-function} % assignment 5, exercise 1
Let $x_1 < \dots < x_n$ be real numbers and $p_1, \ldots, p_n \geq 0$ with $\sum_{k=1}^n p_k = 1$.
Define 
\[
F(x) \coloneq \sum_{k=1}^n p_k \ind_{[x_k,\infty)}(x)
\]
for $x \in \R$.
Let $\mu_F$ be the Lebesgue-Stieltjes measure (\autoref{ex:lebesgue-stieltjes-measure}) associated with $F$.
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item Show that $F$
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item is monotonically increasing
\item is right continuous
\item satisfies $\lim_{x \to -\infty} F(x) = 0$ and $\lim_{x \to \infty} F(x) = 1$
\end{enumerate}
\item Compute $\mu_F((a, b])$ explicitly in terms of the points $x_k$ and the weights $p_k$.
\item Show that $\mu_F(\{x_k\}) = p_k$ for $k = 1, \ldots, n$.
\item Show that if $A \in \mathcal{B}(\R)$, then 
\[
\mu_F(A) = \sum_{k=1}^n p_k \ind_A(x_k)
\]
\item Conclude that every finitely supported probability measure on $\R$, that is, every probability measure of the form
\[
\mu = \sum_{k=1}^n p_k \delta_{x_k}
\]
for some $x_1 < \dots < x_n$ and $p_1, \ldots, p_n \geq 0$ with $\sum_{k=1}^n p_k = 1$, is a Lebesgue-Stieltjes measure (\autoref{ex:lebesgue-stieltjes-measure}).
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
\leavevmode
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item 
Let $x \le y$. 
Then 
\[
\ind_{[x_k,\infty)}(x) \le \ind_{[x_k,\infty)}(y)
\]
for every $k \in \{1,\dots,n\}$.
Since $p_k \ge 0$ for all $k$, summing over $k$ yields $F(x) \le F(y)$. 
Hence $F$ is monotonically increasing.

\item Each function $\ind_{[x_k,\infty)}$ is right continuous. 
Indeed, if $y_m \searrow x$, then:
if $x < x_k$, eventually $y_m < x_k$, so $\ind_{[x_k,\infty)}(y_m)=0$;
if $x \ge x_k$, then $y_m \ge x_k$ for all $m$, so $\ind_{[x_k,\infty)}(y_m)=1$.
\rlap{Thus}
\[
\lim_{m\to\infty} \ind_{[x_k,\infty)}(y_m)=\ind_{[x_k,\infty)}(x)
\]
A finite sum of right continuous functions is right continuous, so $F$ is right continuous.

\item 
If $x \to -\infty$, then for every $k$ we eventually have $x < x_k$, hence
\(\ind_{[x_k,\infty)}(x)=0\).
Therefore $F(x)=0$ eventually, and so $\lim_{x\to -\infty} F(x)=0$.
If $x \to \infty$, then for every $k$ we eventually have $x \ge x_k$, hence
\(\ind_{[x_k,\infty)}(x)=1\).
Therefore $F(x)=\sum_{k=1}^n p_k=1$ eventually, and so $\lim_{x\to \infty} F(x)=1$.
\end{enumerate}

\item 
% By the defining property of the Lebesgue--Stieltjes measure associated with $F$,
% \(\mu_F((a,b]) = F(b)-F(a)\).
Using the definition of $F$, we obtain
\[
\mu_F((a,b]) = \sum_{k=1}^n p_k\bigl(\ind_{[x_k,\infty)}(b)-\ind_{[x_k,\infty)}(a)\bigr).
\]
For fixed $k$,
\[
\ind_{[x_k,\infty)}(b)-\ind_{[x_k,\infty)}(a)
=
\begin{cases}
1 & \text{if } a < x_k \le b\\
0 & \text{otherwise}
\end{cases}
\]
Hence
\(\mu_F((a,b]) = \sum_{k: a < x_k \le b} p_k\).

\item Fix $k \in \{1,\dots,n\}$. Choose $\varepsilon>0$ such that
\((x_k-\varepsilon,x_k] \cap \{x_1,\dots,x_n\} = \{x_k\}\).
Then by part (b),
\(\mu_F((x_k-\varepsilon,x_k]) = \sum_{j: x_k-\varepsilon < x_j \le x_k} p_j = p_k\).
Moreover, $F$ is constant on $(x_k-\varepsilon,x_k)$, since this interval contains no point $x_j$. Hence for every $u,v$ with $x_k-\varepsilon < u \le v < x_k$ we have
\(\mu_F((u,v]) = F(v)-F(u)=0\).
Therefore \(\mu_F((x_k-\varepsilon,x_k))=0\).
Since
\((x_k-\varepsilon,x_k] = (x_k-\varepsilon,x_k) \sqcup \{x_k\}\),
additivity yields
\[
\mu_F((x_k-\varepsilon,x_k])
=
\mu_F((x_k-\varepsilon,x_k)) + \mu_F(\{x_k\})
=
\mu_F(\{x_k\}).
\]
Consequently, $\mu_F(\{x_k\})=p_k$.

\item Define a probability measure $\mu$ on $(\mathbb{R},\mathcal{B}(\mathbb{R}))$ by
\(\mu := \sum_{k=1}^n p_k\delta_{x_k}\).
Then for every Borel set $A \subseteq \mathbb{R}$,
\(\mu(A)=\sum_{x_k\in A} p_k\).
Moreover, for every $a \le b$,
\(\mu((a,b]) = \sum_{k: a < x_k \le b} p_k\).
By part (b),
\(\mu((a,b]) = \mu_F((a,b])\)
for all $a \le b$.
Since both $\mu$ and $\mu_F$ are finite Borel measures on $\mathbb{R}$ and the family
\(\mathcal{H} := \{(a,b] : a,b \in \mathbb{R},\ a \le b\}\)
generates $\mathcal{B}(\mathbb{R})$, uniqueness in the Lebesgue--Stieltjes construction implies that $\mu=\mu_F$. Therefore, for every $A \in \mathcal{B}(\mathbb{R})$,
\(\mu_F(A)=\mu(A)=\sum_{x_k\in A} p_k = \sum_{k=1}^n p_k\ind_A(x_k)\).

\item Let $\nu$ be a finitely supported probability measure on $\mathbb{R}$. Then there exist real numbers $x_1 < \dots < x_n$ and numbers $p_1,\dots,p_n \ge 0$ with $\sum_{k=1}^n p_k = 1$ such that
\(\nu = \sum_{k=1}^n p_k\delta_{x_k}\).
Define
\(F(x) := \sum_{k=1}^n p_k\ind_{[x_k,\infty)}(x)\).
By part (d), the Lebesgue--Stieltjes measure $\mu_F$ associated with $F$ satisfies
\(\mu_F(A)=\sum_{x_k\in A} p_k = \nu(A)\)
for all $A \in \mathcal{B}(\mathbb{R})$.
Hence $\nu = \mu_F$.
Thus every finitely supported probability measure on $(\mathbb{R},\mathcal{B}(\mathbb{R}))$ is a Lebesgue--Stieltjes measure.
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{solution}





\subsection{Null sets}

If $(\Omega,\mathcal{F},\Prob)$ is a probability space, we might expect that every subset of an event $E$ with $\Prob(E)=0$ also has probability zero.
In general, however, subsets of such events need not be measurable.

\begin{definition}\label{def:null-sets}
Let $(\Omega,\mathcal{F},\mu)$ be a measure space.
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
  \item 
  A set $B \in \mathcal{F}$ with $\mu(B)=0$ is called a $\mu$-null set.
  By 
  \[
  \mathcal{N}_\mu \coloneq \{N \subseteq B : B \in \mathcal{F}, \mu(B) = 0\}
  \]
  we denote the family of all subsets of $\mu$-null sets.
  \item 
  Let $P(\omega)$ be a property of $\omega \in \Omega$.
  We say that $P$ holds $\mu$-almost everywhere (a.e.) if there exists a null set $N$ such that $P(\omega)$ holds for every $\omega \in \Omega \setminus N$.
  \item 
  If $\mu=\Prob$ is a probability measure, we say that $P$ holds $\Prob$-almost surely (a.s.).
  \item 
  The measure space $(\Omega,\mathcal{F},\mu)$ is called complete if $\mathcal{F}$ contains all subsets of $\mu$-null sets.
  \qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{definition}




\begin{theorem}
% [Concrete description of the completion]
\label{thm:concrete-description-completion}
Let $(\Omega,\mathcal{F},\mu)$ be a $\sigma$-finite measure space and define
\(
\mathcal{G} \coloneq \{A \cup N : A \in \mathcal{F},\ N \in \mathcal{N}_\mu\}
\).
Then
\(
\mathcal{G} = \sigma(\mathcal{F} \cup \mathcal{N}_\mu)
\).
In particular, $\mathcal{G}$ is a $\sigma$-algebra.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof} % assignment 5, exercise 2
First note that if $N \in \mathcal{N}_\mu$, then by \autoref{def:null-sets} there exists a measurable $\mu$-null set $B \in \mathcal{F}$ such that $N \subseteq B$.

We show that $\mathcal{G}$ is a $\sigma$-algebra:
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item
Since $\emptyset \in \mathcal{N}_\mu$ and $\Omega \in \mathcal{F}$, we have $\Omega = \Omega \cup \emptyset \in \mathcal{G}$.
\cmark
\item
Now let $G = A \cup N \in \mathcal{G}$ with $A \in \mathcal{F}$ and $N \in \mathcal{N}_\mu$. Choose $B \in \mathcal{F}$ with $\mu(B)=0$ and $N \subseteq B$. Then
\tikzexternaldisable
\[
G^\complement = 
\underbrace{(A \cup B)^\complement}_{\in \mathcal{F}}
\cup 
\underbrace{\bigl(B \setminus (A \cup N)\bigr)\tikzmark{Gcomplement}}_{\substack{\subseteq B \\ \in \mathcal{N}_\mu}}%\tikzmark{Gcomplement}
\]
% Here $(A \cup B)^\complement \in \mathcal{F}$, while $B \setminus (A \cup N) \subseteq B$, hence $B \setminus (A \cup N) \in \mathcal{N}_\mu$. 
\definecolor{complementcolor}{HTML}{\eat #fedb90}%
\begin{tikzpicture}[remember picture,overlay]
\coordinate (GcompCoord) at (pic cs:Gcomplement);
\begin{scope}[
  shift={([xshift=-0.0em]current page text area.east|-GcompCoord)}
]
\begin{scope}[
  xshift=-3.2cm,
  yshift=-1.25cm,
  scale=0.2,
  line cap=round,
  line join=round,
  font=\footnotesize
]

% circles
\def\r{3.2}
\def\cax{5}
\def\cay{4}
\def\cbx{9}
\def\cby{4}
\coordinate (CA) at (\cax,\cay);
\coordinate (CB) at (\cbx,\cby);

% horizontal segment in B
\def\yy{3.15}
\path coordinate (L) at ({\cbx - sqrt(max(0,\r*\r - (\yy-\cby)^2))},\yy);
\path coordinate (R) at ({\cbx + sqrt(max(0,\r*\r - (\yy-\cby)^2))},\yy);

% shaded set: (A \cup B)^complement \cup (B \setminus (A \cup N))
\begin{scope}
\clip (0.0,0.0) rectangle (14.0,8.0);
\fill[complementcolor] (0.0,0.0) rectangle (14.0,8.0);
\fill[white] (CA) circle (\r);
\fill[white] (CB) circle (\r);
\end{scope}
\begin{scope}
% Fill B \ (A \cup N): inside B, outside A, above the N-boundary y=yy.
\clip (CB) circle (\r);
\begin{scope}[even odd rule]
\clip (0.0,0.0) rectangle (14.0,8.0) (CA) circle (\r);
\clip (0,\yy) rectangle (14.0,8.0);
\fill[complementcolor] (0.0,0.0) rectangle (14.0,8.0);
\end{scope}
\end{scope}

% outlines
\draw[semithick] (0.0,0.0) rectangle (14.0,8.0);
\draw[thin] (CA) circle (\r);
\draw[thin] (CB) circle (\r);
\draw[thin] (L) -- (R);

% labels
\node at (0.85,7.15) {$\Omega$};
\node at ($(CA)+(120:{0.75*\r})$) {$A$};
\node at ($(CB)+(60:{0.75*\r})$) {$B$};
\pgfmathsetmacro{\Nx}{\cbx + 0.0*\r}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\Ny}{((\cby-\r) + 0.5*(\yy - (\cby-\r)))}
\node at (\Nx,\Ny) {$N$};

\end{scope}
\end{scope}
\end{tikzpicture}
\tikzexternalenable
Therefore $G^\complement \in \mathcal{G}$.
\cmark
\item
Consider $(G_i)_{i \in \mathbb{N}}$ with $G_i = A_i \cup N_i \in \mathcal{G}$ for $i \in \mathbb{N}$ with $A_i \in \mathcal{F}$ and $N_i \in \mathcal{N}_\mu$. 
Choose measurable $\mu$-null sets $B_i \in \mathcal{F}$ such that $N_i \subseteq B_i$. 
Then
\[
\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} G_i 
= 
\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} \left(A_i \cup N_i\right)
=
\left(\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} A_i\right) \cup \left(\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} N_i\right)
\]
By \autoref{def:sigma-algebra}, $\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} A_i \in \mathcal{F}$. 
Since $N_i \subseteq B_i$ for all $i$, we have
\[
\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} N_i \subseteq \bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} B_i
\]
and by countable subadditivity from \autoref{thm:countable-subadditivity},
\[
\mu\left(\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} B_i\right) \leq \sum_{i=1}^{\infty} \mu(B_i) = 0
\]
implying $\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} N_i \in \mathcal{N}_\mu$.
Thus $\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} G_i \in \mathcal{G}$. 
\cmark
\end{enumerate}
Hence $\mathcal{G}$ is a $\sigma$-algebra.

Since $A = A \cup \emptyset \in \mathcal{G}$ for every $A \in \mathcal{F}$ and $N = \emptyset \cup N \in \mathcal{G}$ for every $N \in \mathcal{N}_\mu$, we have
\[
\mathcal{F} \cup \mathcal{N}_\mu \subseteq \mathcal{G}
\]
As $\mathcal{G}$ is a $\sigma$-algebra, \autoref{thm:generated-sigma-algebra} yields
\[
\sigma(\mathcal{F} \cup \mathcal{N}_\mu) \subseteq \mathcal{G}
\]
Conversely, if $G \in \mathcal{G}$, then $G = A \cup N$ with $A \in \mathcal{F}$ and $N \in \mathcal{N}_\mu$. Since both $A$ and $N$ belong to $\mathcal{F} \cup \mathcal{N}_\mu$, they belong to $\sigma(\mathcal{F} \cup \mathcal{N}_\mu)$ by \autoref{thm:generated-sigma-algebra}. As this is a $\sigma$-algebra, it is closed under unions, hence $G \in \sigma(\mathcal{F} \cup \mathcal{N}_\mu)$. Therefore
\[
\mathcal{G} \subseteq \sigma(\mathcal{F} \cup \mathcal{N}_\mu)
\]
Combining both inclusions gives the claim.
\end{proof}



We have the following fact, which states that every $\sigma$-finite measure space admits a completion.

\begin{fact}
Let $(\Omega,\mathcal{F},\mu)$ be a $\sigma$-finite measure space.
Then there exist a unique smallest $\sigma$-algebra $\mathcal{F}^* \supset \mathcal{F}$ and an extension $\mu^*$ of $\mu$ to $\mathcal{F}^*$ such that $(\Omega,\mathcal{F}^*,\mu^*)$ is complete.
The measure space $(\Omega,\mathcal{F}^*,\mu^*)$ is called the completion of $(\Omega,\mathcal{F},\mu)$.
Moreover,
\[
\mathcal{F}^* = \sigma\left(\mathcal{F} \cup \mathcal{N}_\mu\right)
\quad\text{and}\quad
\mu^*(A \cup N) = \mu(A)
\]
for any $A \in \mathcal{F}$ and $N \in \mathcal{N}_\mu$.
\end{fact}




\begin{example}
Let $\lambda^d$ be the Lebesgue-Borel measure on $(\R^d,\mathcal{B}(\R^d))$.
Then $\lambda^d$ can be uniquely extended to a measure $\lambda^*$ on
\[
\mathcal{B}^*(\R^d) = \sigma(\mathcal{B}(\R^d) \cup \mathcal{N})
\]
where $\mathcal{N}$ is the family of all subsets of Lebesgue-Borel null sets.
The $\sigma$-algebra $\mathcal{B}^*(\R^d)$ is called the $\sigma$-algebra of Lebesgue measurable sets.
In what follows, we call $\lambda^d$ the Lebesgue-Borel measure and $\lambda^*$ the Lebesgue measure.
\end{example}




\clearpage


\section{Random variables}


\subsection{Measurable functions}


\begin{definition}\label{def:measurable-function}
Let $(\Omega,\mathcal{F})$ and $(\Omega',\mathcal{F}')$ be measurable spaces (see \autoref{def:sigma-algebra}).
A function $f : \Omega \to \Omega'$ is called \hl{$(\mathcal{F},\mathcal{F}')$-measurable} or simply \emph{measurable} if
\[
f^{-1}(B) 
\coloneq
\{\omega \in \Omega : f(\omega) \in B\}
\in 
\mathcal{F}
\]
for every $B \in \mathcal{F}'$,
i.e. the \hl[2]{preimage} of every measurable set is measurable.
\end{definition}
\begin{definition}\label{def:random-variable}
If $(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \Prob)$ is a probability space and $X : \Omega \to \R$ is $(\mathcal{F}, \mathcal{B}(\R))$-measurable, we call $X$ a \hl[1]{\emph{random variable}} (see diagram).
If $\vect{X} : \Omega \to \R^d$ is $(\mathcal{F}, \mathcal{B}(\R^d))$-measurable, we call $\vect{X}$ a \emph{random vector}.
\end{definition}


\[
  \def\geomscale{0.7}
    \begin{tikzpicture}[font=\footnotesize]
        % Global geometric scale: affects coordinates, node sizes, and shifts,
        % but not line width or font size.
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\u}{\geomscale cm}

        % Geometry lengths
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\SigmaW}{5*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\SigmaH}{6*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\OmegaW}{2*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\OmegaH}{2*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\AW}{1.5*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\AH}{1.5*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\EmptyW}{0.5*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\EmptyH}{0.5*\u}

        % Shifts
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\OmegaShiftY}{1.5*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\wOneShiftX}{-0.2*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\wOneShiftY}{0.5*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\wTwoShiftX}{0.6*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\wTwoShiftY}{0*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\wThreeShiftX}{-0.2*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\wThreeShiftY}{-0.5*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\AShiftX}{1.3*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\AShiftY}{-0.6*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\EmptyShiftX}{-0.5*\u}
        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\EmptyShiftY}{-2*\u}

        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\EShiftY}{0.5*\u}

        \pgfmathsetlengthmacro{\BShiftY}{-1.2*\u}

        \node[below left=0mm of omega] {$\Omega$};

        \node[fill=black, circle, inner sep=1.0pt, outer sep=0pt] (w1) at ([xshift=\wOneShiftX, yshift=\wOneShiftY]omega.center) {};

        \node[fill=black, circle, inner sep=1.0pt, outer sep=0pt] (w3) at ([xshift=\wThreeShiftX, yshift=\wThreeShiftY]omega.center) {};
        \node[below right =-3pt of w1] {\small $\omega_1$};
        \node[left = -1.8pt of w2] {\small $\omega_2$};
        \node[left=-1.8pt of w3] {\small $\omega_3$};
        
        % Define subsets
        \node[draw, semithick, ellipse, minimum width=\AW, minimum height=\AH] (A) at ([xshift=\AShiftX, yshift=\AShiftY]sigmafield.center) {};
        \node[below left=-1mm of A] {$X^{-1}(B)$};
        
        % Single event in A
        \node[fill=black, circle, inner sep=1.0pt, outer sep=0pt] (w4) at (A.center) {};
        \node[below left=-3pt of w4] {\small $\omega_1$};
        
        % Empty set
        \node[draw, semithick, ellipse, minimum width=\EmptyW, minimum height=\EmptyH] (emptyset) at ([xshift=\EmptyShiftX, yshift=\EmptyShiftY]sigmafield.center) {};
        \node[below left = -1.8pt of emptyset] {$\emptyset$};
        \end{scope}

        % Borel sets
        \node[draw, semithick, ellipse, minimum width=\SigmaW, minimum height=\SigmaW] (epsilon) at (\EpsX, \EpsY){};
        \node[right=0mm of epsilon] {$\mathcal{B}(\mathbb{R})$};

        \node[draw, semithick, ellipse, minimum width=\OmegaW, minimum height=\OmegaH] (E) at ([xshift=\EShiftX, yshift=\EShiftY]epsilon.center) {};

        \coordinate (start_R) at (\StartRX, \StartRY);

        \let\lineangle\pgfmathresult
        \pgfmathsetmacro{\perpangle}{\lineangle + 90}
        \path[->, semithick] (A)     edge[out=55, in=190] node[above left] { ${X}$} (B);
        \path[-> , semithick] (B)    edge[out=230, in=15] node[below right] { ${X^{-1}}$} (A);
        \path[->, semithick] (A)     edge[out=-60, in=\perpangle] node[above] { ${\Prob}$} (p_A);
        \path[->, semithick, red] (B)     edge[out=-40, in=\perpangle] node[above right] {$\Prob_{X} = \Prob \circ X^{-1}$} (p_A);

    \end{tikzpicture}
\]


The concept of random variables ensures that we can assign probabilities to sets of the form
\[
\{\omega \in \Omega : X(\omega) \in B\} = X^{-1}(B)
\]
where $B \in \mathcal{B}(\R)$ is a Borel set.
The probability of the above set is given by 
% $\Prob(\{\omega \in \Omega : X(\omega) \in B\})$.
% $\Prob(X \in B)$.
$\Prob(X^{-1}(B))$.
\begin{notation}
As shorthand notation, we often write 
% $\{X(\omega) \in B\}$ or 
$\{X \in B\}$ for $X^{-1}(B) = \{\omega \in \Omega : X(\omega) \allowbreak \in \allowbreak B\}$, and 
% $\Prob(X(\omega) \in B)$ or 
$\Prob(X \in B)$ for $\Prob(X^{-1}(B))$.
\end{notation}

\begin{example}
If $\mathcal{F}$ is the trivial $\sigma$-algebra $\{\varnothing, \Omega\}$, then a function
$X : \Omega \to \Omega'$ is measurable if and only if
\(
X^{-1}(B)\in\{\varnothing,\Omega\}
\)
for every $B\in\mathcal{F}'$.

In particular, if $\Omega'=\mathbb{R}$ and $\mathcal{F}'=\mathcal{B}(\mathbb{R})$, then the only measurable functions $X:\Omega\to\mathbb{R}$ are the constant functions:
% \begin{proof}
if $X$ were not constant, there would be $\omega_1,\omega_2\in\Omega$ with $X(\omega_1)\neq X(\omega_2)$, and then the Borel set $\{X(\omega_1)\}$ would have preimage neither $\varnothing$ nor $\Omega$, contradicting measurability.
% \end{proof}

If $\mathcal{F}$ is the power set $2^\Omega$, then every function $X : \Omega \to \Omega'$ is measurable.
\end{example}

\begin{theorem}
Let $(\Omega,\mathcal{F})$ be a measurable space (\autoref{def:sigma-algebra}) and $A \subseteq \Omega$.
The indicator function
\[
\ind_A(\omega)
=
\begin{cases}
1 & \text{if } \omega \in A \\
0 & \text{otherwise}
\end{cases}
\]
is $(\mathcal{F}, \mathcal{B}(\R))$-measurable if and only if $A \in \mathcal{F}$.
\end{theorem}
\begin{proof}
Consider $B \in \mathcal{B}(\R)$. 
We have
\[
\ind_A^{-1}(B)
=
\begin{cases}
\varnothing & \text{if } 0,1 \notin B \\
A & \text{if } 0 \notin B, 1 \in B \\
A^{\complement} & \text{if } 0 \in B, 1 \notin B \\
\Omega & \text{if } 0,1 \in B
\end{cases}
\]
If $A \in \mathcal{F}$, then the four possible preimages are all in $\mathcal{F}$, so $\ind_A$ is measurable.
If $A \notin \mathcal{F}$, then the preimage of $B = \{1\}$ is $A$, which is not in $\mathcal{F}$, so $\ind_A$ is not measurable.
\end{proof}

\begin{example}
Consider the indicator function $\ind_{\Q} : \R \to \R$ of the rational numbers $\Q \subset \R$.
Since $\Q$ is countable, we have $\Q \in \mathcal{B}(\R)$.
Hence $\ind_{\Q}^{-1}(\{1\}) = \Q$ and $\ind_{\Q}^{-1}(\{0\}) = \R \setminus \Q$ are Borel sets, so $\ind_{\Q}$ is measurable.
\end{example}




\begin{fact}[Properties of preimage]\label{fact:preimage-properties} % assignment 5, problem 3 
Let 
% $(\Omega,\mathcal{F})$, $(\Omega',\mathcal{F}')$, $(\Omega'',\mathcal{F}'')$ be measurable spaces (\autoref{def:sigma-algebra}) and 
$f : \Omega \to \Omega'$ and $g : \Omega' \to \Omega''$ be functions.
Define $h \coloneq g \circ f$.
Then
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item  % assignment 5, problem 3 a)
\label{item:preimage-properties-complement}
$f^{-1}(A^{\complement}) = (f^{-1}(A))^{\complement}$ for any $A \subseteq \Omega'$
\item  % assignment 5, problem 3 b)
\label{item:preimage-properties-union-intersection}
Let $(A_i)_{i \in I}$ be a family of subsets of $\Omega'$. Then
\[
f^{-1}\left(\bigcup_{i \in I} A_i\right) = \bigcup_{i \in I} f^{-1}(A_i)
\qquad
\text{and}
\qquad
f^{-1}\left(\bigcap_{i \in I} A_i\right) = \bigcap_{i \in I} f^{-1}(A_i)
\]
\item % assignment 5, problem 3 c)
\label{item:preimage-properties-composition}
$h^{-1}(B) = f^{-1}(g^{-1}(B))$ for any $B \subseteq \Omega''$
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{fact}

\begin{proof}
% \leavevmode
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item 
$
x \in f^{-1}(A^{\complement})
\Leftrightarrow
f(x) \in A^{\complement}
\Leftrightarrow
f(x) \notin A
\Leftrightarrow
x \notin f^{-1}(A)
\Leftrightarrow
x \in (f^{-1}(A))^{\complement}
$
\item
\begingroup
{\fontsize{7}{10}\selectfont
$
x \in f^{-1}\left(\bigcup\limits_{i \in I} A_i\right)
\Leftrightarrow
f(x) \in \bigcup\limits_{i \in I} A_i
\Leftrightarrow
\exists i \in I : f(x) \in A_i
\Leftrightarrow
\exists i \in I : x \in f^{-1}(A_i)
\Leftrightarrow
x \in \bigcup\limits_{i \in I} f^{-1}(A_i)
$
}
\endgroup
\\
and\\
\begingroup
{\fontsize{7}{10}\selectfont
$
x \in f^{-1}\left(\bigcap\limits_{i \in I} A_i\right)
\Leftrightarrow
f(x) \in \bigcap\limits_{i \in I} A_i
\Leftrightarrow
\forall i \in I : f(x) \in A_i
\Leftrightarrow
\forall i \in I : x \in f^{-1}(A_i)
\Leftrightarrow
x \in \bigcap\limits_{i \in I} f^{-1}(A_i)
$
}
\endgroup
\item
$
x \in h^{-1}(B)
\Leftrightarrow
h(x) \in B
\Leftrightarrow
g(f(x)) \in B
\Leftrightarrow
f(x) \in g^{-1}(B)
\Leftrightarrow
x \in f^{-1}(g^{-1}(B))
$
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{proof}





\subsection{Construction of measurable functions}



\begin{theorem}\label{thm:composition-of-measurable-functions}  % assignment 5, problem 3 d)
Let 
$(\Omega,\mathcal{F})$, $(\Omega',\mathcal{F}')$, $(\Omega'',\mathcal{F}'')$ be measurable spaces (\autoref{def:sigma-algebra}). 
Assume 
$f : \Omega \to \Omega'$ is $(\mathcal{F},\mathcal{F}')$-measurable
and 
$g : \Omega' \to \Omega''$ is $(\mathcal{F}',\mathcal{F}'')$-measurable.
Define $h \coloneq g \circ f$.
Then $h : \Omega \to \Omega''$ is $(\mathcal{F},\mathcal{F}'')$-measurable.
\end{theorem}
\begin{proof}
Let $B \in \mathcal{F}''$.
Since $g$ is $(\mathcal{F}',\mathcal{F}'')$-measurable, we have $g^{-1}(B) \in \mathcal{F}'$.
Since $f$ is $(\mathcal{F},\mathcal{F}')$-measurable, we have $f^{-1}(g^{-1}(B)) \in \mathcal{F}$.
Hence, by \autoref{fact:preimage-properties}.\ref{item:preimage-properties-composition}, $h^{-1}(B) \in \mathcal{F}$.
% Since $B \in \mathcal{F}''$ was arbitrary, $h$ is $(\mathcal{F},\mathcal{F}'')$-measurable.
\end{proof}


\autoref{thm:function-measurable-iff-generator} provides an effective way to check measurability of a function (\autoref{def:measurable-function}).
\begin{fancytheorem}\label{thm:function-measurable-iff-generator}
  Let $(\Omega,\mathcal{F})$, $(\Omega',\mathcal{F}')$ be measurable spaces (\autoref{def:sigma-algebra}) and $f : \Omega \to \Omega'$ be a function.
  Let $\mathcal{A}$ be a generator of $\mathcal{F}'$, i.e. $\sigma(\mathcal{A}) = \mathcal{F}'$ (\autoref{thm:generated-sigma-algebra}).
  Then $f$ is $(\mathcal{F},\mathcal{F}')$-mea\-sur\-a\-ble if and only if $f^{-1}(A) \in \mathcal{F}$ for every $A \in \mathcal{A}$.
\end{fancytheorem}

\begin{proof}
`$\Rightarrow$':
If $f$ is $(\mathcal{F},\mathcal{F}')$-measurable, then $f^{-1}(A) \in \mathcal{F}$ for every $A \in \mathcal{A} \subset \mathcal{F}'$.

`$\Leftarrow$':
%
% what we need to do:
% show that for every set in $\mathcal{F}'$, the preimage is in $\mathcal{F}$.
%
% we assume that for every set in the generator $\mathcal{A}$ (of $\mathcal{F}'$), the preimage is in $\mathcal{F}$.
% but how can we deduce that this stays true when we generate $\mathcal{F}'$ with $\mathcal{A}$, i.e. when we consider $\sigma(\mathcal{A}) = \mathcal{F}'$?
%
% well it kinda makes sense; 
% if it is true for all the sets in $\mathcal{A}$, then 
Assume $f^{-1}(A) \in \mathcal{F}$ for all $A \in \mathcal{A}$.
Consider
\[
\mathcal{C} 
\coloneq
\{B \subseteq \Omega' : f^{-1}(B) \in \mathcal{F}\}
\]
i.e. the collection of subsets of $\Omega'$ whose pre\-images are measurable.
By construction, $\mathcal{A} \subseteq \mathcal{C}$.
It is therefore enough to show that $\mathcal{C}$ is a $\sigma$-algebra (\autoref{def:sigma-algebra}), since by \autoref{thm:generated-sigma-algebra} we then have $\mathcal{F}' = \sigma(\mathcal{A}) \subseteq \mathcal{C}$.
\begin{itemize}
\item 
$\Omega' \in \mathcal{C}$, since $f^{-1}(\Omega') = \Omega \in \mathcal{F}$.
\cmark
\item
Consider $B \in \mathcal{C}$.
By definition, $f^{-1}(B) \in \mathcal{F}$.
Since $\mathcal{F}$ is a $\sigma$-algebra, we have $(f^{-1}(B))^{\complement} \in \mathcal{F}$.
% By \autoref{fact:preimage-properties}.\ref{item:preimage-properties-complement},
% \[
% f^{-1}(B^{\complement}) = (f^{-1}(B))^{\complement}
% \]
% and therefore $f^{-1}(B^{\complement}) \in \mathcal{F}$.
% Hence $B^{\complement} \in \mathcal{C}$.
Therefore, by \autoref{fact:preimage-properties}.\ref{item:preimage-properties-complement}, $f^{-1}(B^{\complement}) \in \mathcal{F}$.
Hence $B^{\complement} \in \mathcal{C}$.
\cmark
\item
Now let $(B_i)_{i=1}^{\infty} \subseteq \mathcal{C}$.
Then $f^{-1}(B_i) \in \mathcal{F}$ for every $i \in \mathbb{N}$.
Since $\mathcal{F}$ is a $\sigma$-algebra, we have $\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} f^{-1}(B_i) \in \mathcal{F}$.
% By \autoref{fact:preimage-properties}.\ref{item:preimage-properties-union-intersection},
% \[
% f^{-1}\left(\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} B_i\right)
% =
% \bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} f^{-1}(B_i)
% \]
% and therefore $f^{-1}(\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} B_i) \in \mathcal{F}$.
% Hence $\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} B_i \in \mathcal{C}$.
Therefore, by \autoref{fact:preimage-properties}.\ref{item:preimage-properties-union-intersection}, $f^{-1}(\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} B_i) \in \mathcal{F}$.
Hence $\bigcup_{i=1}^{\infty} B_i \in \mathcal{C}$.
\cmark
\end{itemize}
Thus, $\mathcal{C}$ is a $\sigma$-algebra.
As mentioned, this implies that 
% $\sigma(\mathcal{A}) = \mathcal{F}' \subseteq \mathcal{C}$,
% hence 
$f$ is $(\mathcal{F}, \mathcal{F}')$-measurable.
\end{proof}

Recall the definition of (topologically) continuous functions from \autoref{def:topological-continuity}.
As the Borel $\sigma$-algebra is generated by a topology, we immediately have the following result on the measurability of continuous functions.
\begin{corollary}\label{cor:continuous-functions-are-measurable}
A continuous function $f : \Omega \to \Omega'$ (\autoref{def:topological-continuity}) between topological spaces $(\Omega, \tau)$ and $(\Omega', \tau')$ is $(\mathcal{B}(\Omega), \mathcal{B}(\Omega'))$-measurable, where $\mathcal{B}(\Omega) = \sigma(\tau)$ and $\mathcal{B}(\Omega') = \sigma(\tau')$. % are the Borel $\sigma$-algebras on $\Omega$ and $\Omega'$, respectively.
\end{corollary}
\begin{proof}
Since $f$ is continuous (\autoref{def:topological-continuity}), we have
\(
f^{-1}(U) \in \tau \subseteq \mathcal{B}(\Omega)
\)
for every open set $U \in \tau'$.
But $\tau'$ generates the Borel $\sigma$-algebra on $\Omega'$, i.e.
\(
\mathcal{B}(\Omega') = \sigma(\tau')
\) (see \autoref{def:borel-sigma-field}).
Hence, by \autoref{thm:function-measurable-iff-generator}, it follows that $f$ is $(\mathcal{B}(\Omega),\mathcal{B}(\Omega'))$-measurable.
\end{proof}
\begin{example}
Any continuous function $f : \R^{d_1} \to \R^{d_2}$ is $(\mathcal{B}(\R^{d_1}), \mathcal{B}(\R^{d_2}))$-measurable.
\end{example}

\begin{theorem}\label{thm:measurable-functions-basic-operations}
Let $(\Omega,\mathcal{F})$ be a measurable space and let $f,g : \Omega \to \R$.
If $f$ and $g$ are $(\mathcal{F}, \allowbreak \mathcal{B}(\R))$-measurable, then so are
\(f+g\), \(f-g\), \(f\cdot g\), \(c \cdot f\) for every $c \in \R$.
If, in addition, $g(\omega)\neq 0$ for all $\omega\in\Omega$, then so is 
\(
{f}/{g} : \Omega \to \R
\).
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Define
\(
\alpha : \Omega \to \R^2
\),
\(
\alpha(\omega)
\coloneq
[
f(\omega),
g(\omega)
]^\top
\).
% We first show that \(\alpha\) is \((\mathcal{F},\mathcal{B}(\R^2))\)-measurable.
For any open rectangle \((a,b)\times(c,d)\subseteq\R^2\), we have
\[
\alpha^{-1}\bigl((a,b)\times(c,d)\bigr)
=
f^{-1}\bigl((a,b)\bigr)\cap g^{-1}\bigl((c,d)\bigr) 
\in 
\mathcal{F}
\]
since (by the measurability of \(f\) and \(g\)) both preimages on the right-hand side belong to \(\mathcal{F}\), and $\mathcal{F}$ is closed under finite intersections.
% Because \(\mathcal{F}\) is closed under finite intersections, it follows that
% \[
% \alpha^{-1}\bigl((a,b)\times(c,d)\bigr)\in\mathcal{F}
% \]
The family of open rectangles generates \(\mathcal{B}(\R^2)\), hence by \autoref{thm:function-measurable-iff-generator}, \(\alpha\) is measurable.

The maps $(x,y)\mapsto x+y$, $x-y$, $xy$, $c\cdot x$, $x/y$ are continuous if we consider $x/y$ only on $\{(x,y):y\neq 0\}$ and, hence, measurable by \autoref{cor:continuous-functions-are-measurable}. 
Thus, by measurability of $\alpha$, \autoref{fact:preimage-properties}.\ref{item:preimage-properties-composition} and \autoref{thm:composition-of-measurable-functions}, the functions $f+g$, $f-g$, $f\cdot g$, $c\cdot f$ and $f/g$ are measurable.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}
It is convenient to include the special symbols $\infty$ and $-\infty$ and to work in the extended real number system $\overline{\R} \coloneq \R \cup \{\pm \infty\}$. 
The operations $\infty-\infty$, $(-\infty)+ \infty$, $\infty / \infty$, $\infty /(-\infty)$, $(-\infty) / \infty$, $(-\infty) /(-\infty)$ are not defined. 
However, by convention, we define $0 \cdot \infty$ and $0 \cdot(-\infty)$ to be equal to $0$. 
% For example, we have
% $$
% x \cdot \infty= \begin{cases}\infty, & \text { if } x>0 \\ -\infty, & \text { if } x<0 \\ 0, & \text { if } x=0\end{cases}
% $$
\end{remark}

\begin{theorem}\label{thm:sup-inf-measurable-functions}
Let $(\Omega,\mathcal{F})$ be a measurable space 
and let
$f_i : \Omega \to \overline{\R}$, $i \in \mathbb{N}$, be a sequence of $(\mathcal{F}, \mathcal{B}(\overline{\R}))$-measurable functions.
Then, 
\[
\inf_{i \in \mathbb{N}} f_i
\quad
\text{and}
\quad
\sup_{i \in \mathbb{N}} f_i
\]
are measurable.
\end{theorem}
\begin{proof}
By \autoref{thm:function-measurable-iff-generator}, it suffices to check the preimages of the generator
\(
\mathcal{A} = \{[-\infty,r] : r \in \mathbb{R}\}
\)
of $\mathcal{B}(\overline{\mathbb{R}})$.
Consider the function
\(
g(\omega) \coloneq \sup_{i \in \mathbb{N}} f_i(\omega)
\).
Let $r \in \mathbb{R}$. 
From the definition of the supremum, we obtain
\[
g^{-1}([-\infty,r])
=
\{\omega \in \Omega : \sup_{i \in \mathbb{N}} f_i(\omega) \le r\}
=
\bigcap_{i=1}^{\infty}
\{\omega \in \Omega : f_i(\omega) \le r\}
=
\bigcap_{i=1}^{\infty} f_i^{-1}([-\infty,r])
\]
Since each $f_i$ is measurable and $[-\infty,r] \in \mathcal{B}(\overline{\mathbb{R}})$, it follows that
\(
f_i^{-1}([-\infty,r]) \in \mathcal{F}
\)
for every $i \in \mathbb{N}$. 
Since the right-hand side is a countable intersection of measurable sets, the left-hand side is also measurable. 
Hence $g^{-1}([-\infty,r]) \in \mathcal{F}$ for every $r \in \mathbb{R}$. 
Therefore, by \autoref{thm:function-measurable-iff-generator}, the supremum $\sup_{i \in \mathbb{N}} f_i$ is measurable.


Similarly, for the infimum, we check the preimages of the generator $\mathcal{A} = \{[r, \infty] : r \in \mathbb{R}\}$ of $\mathcal{B}(\overline{\mathbb{R}})$.
Now consider the function
\(
g(\omega) \coloneq \inf_{i \in \mathbb{N}} f_i(\omega)
\).
Let $r \in \mathbb{R}$.
From the definition of the infimum, we obtain
\[
g^{-1}([r, \infty])
=
\{\omega \in \Omega : \inf_{i \in \mathbb{N}} f_i(\omega) \ge r\}
=
\bigcap_{i=1}^{\infty}\{\omega \in \Omega : f_i(\omega) \ge r\}
=
\bigcap_{i=1}^{\infty} f_i^{-1}([r, \infty])
\]
Since each $f_i$ is measurable and $[r, \infty] \in \mathcal{B}(\overline{\mathbb{R}})$, it follows that
\(
f_i^{-1}([r, \infty]) \in \mathcal{F}
\)
for every $i \in \mathbb{N}$.
Since the right-hand side is a countable intersection of measurable sets, the left-hand side is also measurable.
Hence $g^{-1}([r, \infty]) \in \mathcal{F}$ for every $r \in \mathbb{R}$.
Therefore, by \autoref{thm:function-measurable-iff-generator}, the infimum $\inf_{i \in \mathbb{N}} f_i$ is measurable.

Alternatively, using \autoref{thm:measurable-functions-basic-operations} we could also direcly infer that $\inf_{i \in \mathbb{N}} f_i(\omega)$ is measurable, since
\(
\inf_{i \in \mathbb{N}} f_i(\omega)
=
-\sup_{i \in \mathbb{N}}(-f_i(\omega))
\).
\end{proof}

Recall that for a sequence of real numbers $(x_n)_{n \in \mathbb{N}}$, the limit inferior and limit superior may~be written as
\[
\liminf_{n \to \infty} x_n = \sup_{n \in \mathbb{N}} \inf_{k \ge n} x_k
\qquad
\text{and}
\qquad
\limsup_{n \to \infty} x_n = \inf_{n \in \mathbb{N}} \sup_{k \ge n} x_k
\]
respectively, since the sequence of infima is monotonically increasing and the sequence of suprema is monotonically decreasing.


\[
\begin{tikzpicture}
% Sequence model:
% x_n = mu + (A + B exp(-lambda t_n)) cos(pi t_n) + C exp(-lambda t_n),  t_n = n*dt.
% The red curves are computed (not hardcoded):
% s_n = sup_{m>=n} x_m, i_n = inf_{m>=n} x_m, via backward recursion.
\definecolor{limsupliminfcol}{HTML}{\eat #00a8c6}
\definecolor{supinfcol}{HTML}{\eat #399B00}
\definecolor{sequencecol}{HTML}{\eat #ff8922}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\xMin}{0}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\xMax}{14}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\xTailMax}{\xMax+6} % extend horizon so tail sup/inf near xMax are accurate
\pgfmathsetmacro{\dt}{0.1}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\muSeq}{0.0}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\ASeq}{0.5}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\BSeq}{0.6}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\CSeq}{0.0}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\lambdaSeq}{0.3}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\omegaDeg}{180} % cos(pi t)
\pgfmathtruncatemacro{\Nplot}{round((\xMax-\xMin)/\dt)}
\pgfmathtruncatemacro{\Ncalc}{round((\xTailMax-\xMin)/\dt)}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\LimsupVal}{\muSeq+\ASeq}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\LiminfVal}{\muSeq-\ASeq}

\def\sequenceCoords{}
\def\supCoords{}
\def\infCoords{}

% Sample x_n and store in xval@<n>.
\foreach \n in {0,...,\Ncalc}{
  \pgfmathsetmacro{\tn}{\xMin+\n*\dt}
  \pgfmathsetmacro{\xn}{\muSeq + (\ASeq + \BSeq*exp(-\lambdaSeq*\tn))*cos(\omegaDeg*\tn) + \CSeq*exp(-\lambdaSeq*\tn)}
  \expandafter\xdef\csname xval@\n\endcsname{\xn}
  \ifnum\n>\Nplot\relax\else
    \xdef\sequenceCoords{\sequenceCoords (\tn,\xn)}
  \fi
}

% Backward recursion for tail sup / inf.
\newcount\tailidx
\tailidx=\Ncalc
\pgfmathsetmacro{\tailSup}{\csname xval@\the\tailidx\endcsname}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\tailInf}{\csname xval@\the\tailidx\endcsname}
\expandafter\xdef\csname tailsup@\the\tailidx\endcsname{\tailSup}
\expandafter\xdef\csname tailinf@\the\tailidx\endcsname{\tailInf}
\loop
  \ifnum\tailidx>0
    \advance\tailidx by -1
    \pgfmathsetmacro{\xn}{\csname xval@\the\tailidx\endcsname}
    \pgfmathsetmacro{\tailSup}{max(\xn,\tailSup)}
    \pgfmathsetmacro{\tailInf}{min(\xn,\tailInf)}
    \expandafter\xdef\csname tailsup@\the\tailidx\endcsname{\tailSup}
    \expandafter\xdef\csname tailinf@\the\tailidx\endcsname{\tailInf}
\repeat

% Build coordinate lists for s_n and i_n.
\foreach \n in {0,...,\Nplot}{
  \pgfmathsetmacro{\tn}{\xMin+\n*\dt}
  \pgfmathsetmacro{\sN}{\csname tailsup@\n\endcsname}
  \pgfmathsetmacro{\iN}{\csname tailinf@\n\endcsname}
  \xdef\supCoords{\supCoords (\tn,\sN)}
  \xdef\infCoords{\infCoords (\tn,\iN)}
}

% Arrow anchor points (x chosen visually, y read from computed sequences).
\pgfmathsetmacro{\xSupA}{1.6}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\xSupB}{5.1}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\xSupC}{9.3}
\pgfmathtruncatemacro{\iSupA}{round((\xSupA-\xMin)/\dt)}
\pgfmathtruncatemacro{\iSupB}{round((\xSupB-\xMin)/\dt)}
\pgfmathtruncatemacro{\iSupC}{round((\xSupC-\xMin)/\dt)}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\ySupA}{\csname tailsup@\iSupA\endcsname}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\ySupB}{\csname tailsup@\iSupB\endcsname}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\ySupC}{\csname tailsup@\iSupC\endcsname}

\pgfmathsetmacro{\xInfA}{2.0}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\xInfB}{5.2}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\xInfC}{9.2}
\pgfmathtruncatemacro{\iInfA}{round((\xInfA-\xMin)/\dt)}
\pgfmathtruncatemacro{\iInfB}{round((\xInfB-\xMin)/\dt)}
\pgfmathtruncatemacro{\iInfC}{round((\xInfC-\xMin)/\dt)}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\yInfA}{\csname tailinf@\iInfA\endcsname}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\yInfB}{\csname tailinf@\iInfB\endcsname}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\yInfC}{\csname tailinf@\iInfC\endcsname}

\begin{axis}[
    width=9cm,
    height=6cm,
    xmin=\xMin, xmax=\xMax,
    ymin=-1.4, ymax=1.4,
    set layers=standard,
    mark layer=like plot,
    % axis lines=box,
    axis lines=none,

    ytick=\empty,
    % axis background/.style={fill=gray!15},
    clip=false,
]

% blue sequence values x_n
\addplot[
    only marks,
    mark=*,
    mark size=0.5pt,
    sequencecol,
] coordinates {\sequenceCoords};


%  computed tail sup / inf sequences (discrete points)
\addplot[
    supinfcol,
    only marks,
    mark=*,
