] coordinates {\supCoords};

\addplot[
    supinfcol,
    only marks,
    mark=*,
    mark size=0.35pt,
] coordinates {\infCoords};


% dashed limsup / liminf levels implied by sequence parameters
\addplot[
    limsupliminfcol,
    densely dashed,
    line width=0.7pt,
    on layer=axis foreground,
] coordinates {(\xMin,\LimsupVal) (\xMax+0.4,\LimsupVal)};

\addplot[
    limsupliminfcol,
    densely dashed,
    line width=0.7pt,
    on layer=axis foreground,
] coordinates {(\xMin,\LiminfVal) (\xMax+0.4,\LiminfVal)};


% coordinate axes
\draw[->, on layer=axis foreground, black, line width=0.6pt]
  (axis cs:\xMin,0) -- (axis cs:\xMax+0.8,0)
  node[anchor=west, font=\footnotesize] {$n$};
\draw[->, on layer=axis foreground, black, line width=0.6pt]
  (axis cs:0,-1.35) -- (axis cs:0,1.35)
  node[anchor=east, font=\footnotesize, outer sep=0.2em] {$x_n$};


% labels
\node[limsupliminfcol, font=\footnotesize, inner sep=1pt] (limsupLabel) at (axis cs:10.7,0.92) {$\limsup\limits_{n \to \infty} x_n$};
\node[limsupliminfcol, font=\footnotesize, inner sep=1pt] (liminfLabel) at (axis cs:10.8,-1.08) {$\liminf\limits_{n \to \infty} x_n$};
\node[supinfcol, font=\footnotesize, inner sep=1pt, align=center] (supLabel) at (axis cs:6.5,1.12) {$\sup_{k \ge n}x_k$};
\node[supinfcol, font=\footnotesize, inner sep=1pt, align=center] (infLabel) at (axis cs:5.2,-1.18) {$\inf_{k \ge n}x_k$};
% black arrows to limsup / liminf
\draw[-{Latex[length=1mm]},on layer=axis foreground, limsupliminfcol, line width=0.5pt] ($(limsupLabel.south east) + (-3,8)$) -- (axis cs:12.9,\LimsupVal);
\draw[-{Latex[length=1mm]},on layer=axis foreground, limsupliminfcol, line width=0.5pt] (liminfLabel.north east) -- (axis cs:12.8,\LiminfVal);
% supinfcol arrows to upper step sequence
\draw[-{Latex[length=1mm]},on layer=axis foreground, supinfcol, line width=0.5pt] (supLabel.south west) -- (axis cs:\xSupA,\ySupA);
\draw[-{Latex[length=1mm]},on layer=axis foreground, supinfcol, line width=0.5pt] (supLabel.south) -- (axis cs:\xSupB,\ySupB);
\draw[-{Latex[length=1mm]},on layer=axis foreground, supinfcol, line width=0.5pt] (supLabel.south east) -- (axis cs:\xSupC,\ySupC);
% supinfcol arrows to lower step sequence
\draw[-{Latex[length=1mm]},on layer=axis foreground, supinfcol, line width=0.5pt] (infLabel.north west) -- (axis cs:\xInfA,\yInfA);
\draw[-{Latex[length=1mm]},on layer=axis foreground, supinfcol, line width=0.5pt] (infLabel.north) -- (axis cs:\xInfB,\yInfB);
\draw[-{Latex[length=1mm]},on layer=axis foreground, supinfcol, line width=0.5pt] (infLabel.north east) -- (axis cs:\xInfC,\yInfC);
\end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
\]




Therefore, we may characterize them as follows:
\begin{itemize}
  \item $\liminf_{n \to \infty} x_n$ is the largest $b_{\text{lower}} \in \R$ such that for any $\varepsilon > 0$, there exists $N \in \mathbb{N}$ such that $x_n > b_{\text{lower}} - \varepsilon$ for all $n \ge N$
  \item $\limsup_{n \to \infty} x_n$ is the smallest $b_{\text{upper}} \in \R$ such that for any $\varepsilon > 0$, there exists $N \in \mathbb{N}$ such that $x_n < b_{\text{upper}} + \varepsilon$ for all $n \ge N$
  \item for any sequence $(x_n)_{n \in \mathbb{N}}$, we have $\liminf_{n \to \infty} x_n \le \limsup_{n \to \infty} x_n$, with equality when $\lim_{n \to \infty} x_n$ exists
\end{itemize}



\begin{theorem}
Let $f_i : \Omega \to \overline{\R}$, $i \in \mathbb{N}$, be a sequence of measurable functions.
Then, the functions 
\[
\liminf_{i \to \infty} f_i
\quad
\text{and}
\quad
\limsup_{i \to \infty} f_i
\]
are measurable. 
Furthermore, if $\lim_{i \to \infty} f_i(\omega)$ exists for all $\omega \in \Omega$, 
then 
\(
\lim_{i \to \infty} f_i
\)
is measurable.
\end{theorem}
\begin{proof}
From \autoref{thm:sup-inf-measurable-functions} we know that the supremum and infimum of a sequence of measurable functions are measurable.
By the previous characterization of the limit inferior and limit superior, it follows that 
they % $\liminf_{i \to \infty} f_i$ and $\limsup_{i \to \infty} f_i$ 
are measurable.
If the pointwise limit of $f_i$ exists, then the limit inferior and limit superior coincide, and hence the limit is measurable as well.
\end{proof}


% \[
% \{\omega:f(\omega)\le a\}
% =\bigcap_{n=1}^\infty \{\omega:f(\omega)<a+\tfrac1n\}.
% \]




\begin{proposition} % assignment 6, exercise 2
Let $(\Omega, \mathcal{F})$ be a measurable space and let $f: \Omega \rightarrow \overline{\mathbb{R}}$ be measurable. 
For $n \in \mathbb{N}$, define the truncation
\[
% f_n(\omega):=\max \{-n, \min \{f(\omega), n\}\}
f_n(\omega) 
\coloneq 
\begin{cases}
  -n & \text{if } f(\omega) < -n \\
  f(\omega) & \text{if } -n \leq f(\omega) \leq n \\
  n & \text{if } f(\omega) > n
\end{cases}
\]
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item
Show that $f_n: \Omega \rightarrow \mathbb{R}$ is measurable for every $n \in \mathbb{N}$.
\item
Show that
\(
\left|f_n(\omega)\right| \leq n 
\)
for all
\(
\omega \in \Omega
\).
\item
Show that if $f(\omega) \in \mathbb{R}$, then
\(
f_n(\omega) \rightarrow f(\omega)
\) 
as
\(
 n \rightarrow \infty 
\).
\item
Describe the behavior of $f_n(\omega)$ when $f(\omega)=\infty$ and when $f(\omega)=-\infty$.
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{proposition}
% \begin{proof}
% \begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
% \item
% Let $a,b \in \R$ with $a < b$.
% We have
% \[
% f_n^{-1}([a,b])
% =
% \begin{cases}
% \varnothing & \text{if }  n < a \text{ or } b < -n  \\
% \Omega & \text{if } a \leq -n \text{ and }  n \leq b\\
% f^{-1}([-\infty, b]) & \text{if } a \leq -n \text{ and } b < n \\
% f^{-1}([a, b]) & \text{if } -n < a \text{ and } b < n \\
% f^{-1}([a, +\infty]) & \text{if } -n < a \text{ and }  n \leq b \\
% \end{cases}
% \]
% \end{enumerate}
% \end{proof}

\begin{proof}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item
% We show that $f_n : \Omega \to \R$ is measurable by checking the preimages of the generator
We employ \autoref{thm:function-measurable-iff-generator} to check the preimages of the generator
\[
\mathcal{A}
=
\{[a,b] : a,b \in \R,\ a \le b\}
\]
of $\mathcal{B}(\R)$.
Let $a,b \in \R$ with $a \le b$.
Since $f_n(\omega) \in [-n,n]$ for every $\omega \in \Omega$,
we have
\[
f_n^{-1}([a,b])
=
\begin{cases}
\varnothing & \text{if } b < -n \text{ or } a > n
\\
\Omega & \text{if } a \le -n \text{ and } b \ge n
\\
f^{-1}([ -\infty, b]) & \text{if } a \le -n \le b < n
\\
f^{-1}([a,b]) & \text{if } -n < a \le b < n
\\
f^{-1}([a,\infty]) & \text{if } -n < a \le n \le b
\end{cases}
\]
% Indeed:
% \begin{itemize}
% \item if $b < -n$ or $a > n$, then $[a,b] \cap [-n,n] = \varnothing$, so the preimage is empty;
% \item if $a \le -n$ and $b \ge n$, then $[-n,n] \subseteq [a,b]$, so the preimage is $\Omega$;
% \item if $a \le -n$ and $-n \le b < n$, then
% \[
% f_n(\omega) \in [a,b]
% \iff
% f_n(\omega) \le b
% \iff
% f(\omega) \le b,
% \]
% so $f_n^{-1}([a,b]) = f^{-1}([ -\infty, b])$;
% \item if $-n < a \le b < n$, then
% \[
% f_n(\omega) \in [a,b]
% \iff
% a \le f(\omega) \le b,
% \]
% so $f_n^{-1}([a,b]) = f^{-1}([a,b])$;
% \item if $-n < a \le n$ and $b \ge n$, then
% \[
% f_n(\omega) \in [a,b]
% \iff
% f_n(\omega) \ge a
% \iff
% f(\omega) \ge a,
% \]
% so $f_n^{-1}([a,b]) = f^{-1}([a,\infty])$.
% \end{itemize}
In every case, $f_n^{-1}([a,b]) \in \mathcal{F}$ because $f$ is $(\mathcal{F}, \mathcal{B}(\overline{\R}))$-measurable.
Hence $f_n$ is measurable.

\item
By definition of $f_n$, we always have
\(
-n \le f_n(\omega) \le n
\).
Therefore
\(
|f_n(\omega)| \le n
\)
for all $\omega \in \Omega$.

\item
Assume that $f(\omega) \in \R$.
Choose $N \in \N$ with $N \ge |f(\omega)|$, e.g. $N = \lceil |f(\omega)| \rceil$.
Then for every $n \ge N$ we have
\[
-n \le f(\omega) \le n
\]
so by the definition of the truncation,
\(
f_n(\omega) = f(\omega)
\)
for every $n \ge N$.
Hence $f_n(\omega) \to f(\omega)$ as $n \to \infty$.

\item
If $f(\omega) = \infty$, then $f(\omega) > n$ $\forall n \in \N$, hence
\(
f_n(\omega) = n
\)
$\forall n$.
Thus $f_n(\omega) \to \infty$.

If $f(\omega) = -\infty$, then $f(\omega) < -n$ $\forall n \in \N$, hence
\(
f_n(\omega) = -n
\)
$\forall n$.
Thus $f_n(\omega) \to -\infty$.
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{proof}





\clearpage

\section{Independence}


\subsection{Independent Random Variables}

% \emph{Independence} is a key concept in probability theory and sets it apart from real analysis.
Let $A$ be an event with probability $\Prob(A)$.
If we know that another event $B$ has occurred, we update our information on $A$ by replacing $\Prob(A)$ with the \emph{conditional probability} 
\[
\Prob(A \mid B) = \frac{\Prob(A \cap B)}{\Prob(B)}
\]
provided $\Prob(B) > 0$.
% Note that $B$ occurring is not a sufficient condition for $\Prob(B) > 0$.
Note that an event may occur and still have probability $0$, so this assumption is necessary.

Independence of $A$ and $B$ means that this update has no effect, i.e.
$
\Prob(A \mid B) = \Prob(A)
$
or equivalently
$
\Prob(A \cap B) = \Prob(A)  \Prob(B)
$.
Hence, the occurence of $B$ does not change the likelihood of $A$.

\begin{definition}[Independence of events]
Let $(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \Prob)$ be a probability space.
Two events (see \autoref{def:algebra}) $A, B \in \mathcal{F}$ are \emph{independent} if $\Prob(A \cap B) = \Prob(A) \Prob(B)$.
\end{definition}

\begin{example}
% Some events are always independent, while others are never independent:
\leavevmode
\begin{itemize}
\item always independent: $\Omega$ and $\varnothing$ are independent of any event $A \in \mathcal{F}$, since 
\[
\Prob(\Omega \cap A) = \Prob(A) = \Prob(\Omega) \Prob(A) \quad \text{and} \quad \Prob(\varnothing \cap A) = 0 = \Prob(\varnothing) \Prob(A)
\]
\item never independent: if $0 < \Prob(A) < 1$, then $A$ and $A^{\complement}$ are not independent, since 
\[
\Prob(A \cap A^{\complement}) = 0 \neq \Prob(A) \Prob(A^{\complement})
\qedhere
\]
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

% PATCH: Add label to definition of independence of random variables
\begin{definition}[Independence of random variables]\label{def:independence-of-random-variables}
Let $(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \Prob)$ be a probability space and let $X, Y : \Omega \to \R$ be random variables (see \autoref{def:random-variable}).
We say that $X$ and $Y$ are \emph{independent} if the events $\{X \in A\}$ and $\{Y \in B\}$ are independent for all $A, B \in \mathcal{B}(\R)$, i.e., if
\[
% \Prob(\{\omega \in \Omega : X(\omega) \in A \land Y(\omega) \in B\}) 
% = \Prob(\{\omega \in \Omega : X(\omega) \in A\} \cap \{\omega \in \Omega : Y(\omega) \in B\})
% = \Prob(\{\omega \in \Omega : X(\omega) \in A\}) \Prob(\{\omega \in \Omega : Y(\omega) \in B\})
\Prob(X \in A, Y \in B) = \Prob(X \in A) \Prob(Y \in B)
\]
for all $A, B \in \mathcal{B}(\R)$.
\end{definition}

It is often convenient to rephrase independence in terms of generated $\sigma$-algebras.
\begin{definition}\label{def:sigma-algebra-generated-by-random-variable}
Let $(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \Prob)$ be a probability space and let $X : \Omega \to \R$ be a random variable.
% The $\sigma$-algebra generated by $X$ is
We call
\[
{\color{red}\boxed{\color{black}
\sigma(X) \coloneq \{X^{-1}(B) : B \in \mathcal{B}(\R)\} \subseteq \mathcal{F}
}}
\]
the \hl[2]{\emph{$\sigma$-algebra generated by $X$}}.
It encodes the information carried by $X$. % what is meant by "informatino" here?
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
Let $(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \Prob)$ be a probability space and let $\mathcal{F}_1, \mathcal{F}_2 \subseteq \mathcal{F}$ be two $\sigma$-algebras.
We say that $\mathcal{F}_1$ and $\mathcal{F}_2$ are \emph{independent} if 
\[
\Prob(A_1 \cap A_2) = \Prob(A_1) \Prob(A_2)
\]
for all $A_1 \in \mathcal{F}_1$ and $A_2 \in \mathcal{F}_2$.
\end{definition}

% The following result is immediate:
\begin{theorem}
Two random variables $X$ and $Y$ are independent if and only if the $\sigma$-algebras $\sigma(X)$ and $\sigma(Y)$ are independent.
\end{theorem}

The independence of random variables is already guaranteed, if they are independent on a semiring (\autoref{def:semiring}) generating the Borel $\sigma$-algebra (\autoref{def:borel-sigma-field}).
\begin{theorem}
Let $(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \Prob)$ be a probability space and let $X, Y : \Omega \to \R$ be random variables.
Suppose that the Borel $\sigma$-algebra $\mathcal{B}(\R)$ is generated by a semiring $\mathcal{S} \subseteq 2^{\R}$, i.e. $\sigma(\mathcal{S}) = \mathcal{B}(\R)$.
If 
\[
\Prob(X \in A, Y \in B) = \Prob(X \in A) \Prob(Y \in B)
\]
for all $A, B \in \mathcal{S}$, then $X$ and $Y$ are independent.
\end{theorem}
% PATCH 1: Replace third item in semiring itemize with new text
\begin{proof}
We start by showing that the product $\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S} \coloneq \{S_1 \times S_2 : S_1, S_2 \in \mathcal{S}\}$ is again a semiring (\autoref{def:semiring}):
\vspace{4em}
% \begin{itemize}
% \item 
% $\varnothing = \varnothing \times \varnothing \in \mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S}$. \cmark
% \item 
% If $A_1 \times B_1, A_2 \times B_2 \in \mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S}$, then
% \[
% (A_1 \times B_1) \cap (A_2 \times B_2)
% \overset{\text{\eqref{eq:intersection-of-product}}}{=}
% (A_1 \cap A_2) \times (B_1 \cap B_2)
% \]
% and since $\mathcal{S}$ is a semiring, $A_1 \cap A_2 \in \mathcal{S}$ and $B_1 \cap B_2 \in \mathcal{S}$.
% Hence $(A_1 \times B_1) \cap (A_2 \times B_2) \allowbreak \in \allowbreak \mathcal{S} \allowbreak \times \allowbreak \mathcal{S}$. \cmark
% \item 
% Let $A_1 \times B_1, A_2 \times B_2 \in \mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S}$ with
% \(
% A_2 \times B_2 \subseteq A_1 \times B_1
% \).
% If $A_2 = \varnothing$ or $B_2 = \varnothing$, then $A_2 \times B_2 = \varnothing$, hence
% \[
% (A_1 \times B_1) \setminus (A_2 \times B_2) = A_1 \times B_1 \in \mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S}.
% \]
% Otherwise, choose $a_0 \in A_2$ and $b_0 \in B_2$. Then for every $a \in A_2$ we have
% \(
% (a,b_0) \in A_2 \times B_2 \subseteq A_1 \times B_1
% \),
% so $a \in A_1$.
% Hence $A_2 \subseteq A_1$.
% Similarly, for every $b \in B_2$ we have
% \(
% (a_0,b) \in A_2 \times B_2 \subseteq A_1 \times B_1
% \),
% so $b \in B_1$.
% Hence $B_2 \subseteq B_1$.
% Moreover,
% \[
% (A_1 \times B_1) \setminus (A_2 \times B_2)
% =
% \bigl((A_1 \setminus A_2) \times B_1\bigr)
% \cup
% \bigl(A_2 \times (B_1 \setminus B_2)\bigr)
% \]
% and the union is disjoint.
% Since $\mathcal{S}$ is a semiring, there exist pairwise disjoint sets $C_1,\dots,C_m \in \mathcal{S}$ and $D_1,\dots,D_n \in \mathcal{S}$ such that
% \[
% A_1 \setminus A_2 = \bigsqcup_{i=1}^m C_i
% \qquad\text{and}\qquad
% B_1 \setminus B_2 = \bigsqcup_{j=1}^n D_j.
% \]
% Therefore,
% \[
% (A_1 \times B_1) \setminus (A_2 \times B_2)
% =
% \bigsqcup_{i=1}^m (C_i \times B_1)
% \sqcup
% \bigsqcup_{j=1}^n (A_2 \times D_j),
% \]
% which is a finite disjoint union of sets in $\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S}$. Thus $\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S}$ is a semiring. \cmark
% \end{itemize}

Moreover, we have $\sigma(\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S}) = \sigma(\mathcal{S}) \otimes \sigma(\mathcal{S}) = \mathcal{B}(\R) \otimes \mathcal{B}(\R) = \mathcal{B}(\R^2)$, where $\otimes$ denotes the product $\sigma$-algebra (see \autoref{ex:product-sigma-algebra}):
\vspace{4em}
% Indeed, every rectangle $A \times B$ with $A,B \in \mathcal{S}$ belongs to $\sigma(\mathcal{S}) \otimes \sigma(\mathcal{S})$, so
% \(
% \sigma(\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S}) \subseteq \sigma(\mathcal{S}) \otimes \sigma(\mathcal{S})
% \).
% Conversely, for fixed $B \in \mathcal{S}$, the class
% \[
% \mathcal{C}_B \coloneq \{A \subseteq \R : A \times B \in \sigma(\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S})\}
% \]
% is a $\sigma$-algebra containing $\mathcal{S}$, hence $\sigma(\mathcal{S}) \subseteq \mathcal{C}_B$.
% Thus $A \times B \in \sigma(\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S})$ for all $A \in \sigma(\mathcal{S})$, $B \in \mathcal{S}$.
% Now for fixed $A \in \sigma(\mathcal{S})$, the class
% \[
% \mathcal{D}_A \coloneq \{B \subseteq \R : A \times B \in \sigma(\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S})\}
% \]
% is again a $\sigma$-algebra containing $\mathcal{S}$, hence $\sigma(\mathcal{S}) \subseteq \mathcal{D}_A$.
% Therefore $A \times B \in \sigma(\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S})$ for all $A,B \in \sigma(\mathcal{S})$, which yields
% \(
% \sigma(\mathcal{S}) \otimes \sigma(\mathcal{S}) \subseteq \sigma(\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S})
% \).
% This proves the first equality, and the remaining equalities follow from $\sigma(\mathcal{S})=\mathcal{B}(\R)$ and the standard identity $\mathcal{B}(\R) \otimes \mathcal{B}(\R)=\mathcal{B}(\R^2)$.

We define the set functions $\mu, \mu_{\text{prod}} : \mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S} \to [0,1]$ by
\[
\mu(A \times B) \coloneq \Prob(X \in A, Y \in B)
\qquad
\mu_{\text{prod}}(A \times B) \coloneq \Prob_{X}(A) \Prob_{Y}(B)
\]
where the \hl{image measure} $\Prob_{Z}$ is defined as
\[
\Prob_{Z}(C) \coloneq (\Prob \circ Z^{-1})(C) = \Prob(Z^{-1}(C)) = \Prob(Z \in C)
\]
for any random variable $Z : \Omega \to \R$ and any $C \in \mathcal{B}(\R)$.

By assumption we have $\mu(A \times B) = \mu_{\text{prod}}(A \times B)$ for all $A, B \in \mathcal{S}$.
Both set functions $\mu$ and $\mu_{\text{prod}}$ satisfy the assumptions of the Carathéodory extension \autoref{thm:caratheodory-extension}:
\vspace{4em}
% \begin{itemize}
%   \item 
%   Both satisfy $\mu(\varnothing)=\mu_{\text{prod}}(\varnothing)=0$. \cmark
%   % PATCH 2: Replace additive item with new text
%   \item 
%   Both are additive on $\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S}$: if
%   \[
%   A \times B = \bigsqcup_{k=1}^n (A_k \times B_k)
%   \]
%   with $A \times B, A_k \times B_k \in \mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S}$, then
%   \[
%   \mu(A \times B)
%   =
%   \Prob\left((X,Y) \in \bigsqcup_{k=1}^n (A_k \times B_k)\right)
%   =
%   \sum_{k=1}^n \Prob((X,Y) \in A_k \times B_k)
%   =
%   \sum_{k=1}^n \mu(A_k \times B_k)
%   \]
%   by finite additivity of probability.
%   For $\mu_{\text{prod}}$, refine the finite families $A_1,\dots,A_n$ and $B_1,\dots,B_n$ to pairwise disjoint partitions
%   \[
%   A = \bigsqcup_{r=1}^m C_r
%   \qquad\text{and}\qquad
%   B = \bigsqcup_{s=1}^{\ell} D_s
%   \]
%   with $C_r,D_s \in \mathcal{S}$ such that every rectangle $A_k \times B_k$ is a disjoint union of some of the rectangles $C_r \times D_s$.
%   Then
%   \[
%   \mu_{\text{prod}}(A \times B)
%   =
%   \Prob_X(A)\Prob_Y(B)
%   =
%   \left(\sum_{r=1}^m \Prob_X(C_r)\right)
%   \left(\sum_{s=1}^{\ell} \Prob_Y(D_s)\right)
%   =
%   \sum_{r=1}^m \sum_{s=1}^{\ell} \Prob_X(C_r)\Prob_Y(D_s),
%   \]
%   and regrouping the terms according to the rectangles $A_k \times B_k$ yields
%   \[
%   \mu_{\text{prod}}(A \times B)
%   =
%   \sum_{k=1}^n \mu_{\text{prod}}(A_k \times B_k).
%   \]
%   Hence $\mu_{\text{prod}}$ is additive as well. \cmark
%   % PATCH 3: Replace sigma-subadditive item with new text
%   \item 
%   Both are $\sigma$-subadditive: if
%   \[
%   A \times B \subseteq \bigcup_{k=1}^{\infty} (A_k \times B_k)
%   \]
%   with $A \times B, A_k \times B_k \in \mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S}$, then
%   \[
%   \mu(A \times B)
%   =
%   \Prob\left((X,Y) \in A \times B\right)
%   \leq
%   \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \Prob\left((X,Y) \in A_k \times B_k\right)
%   =
%   \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \mu(A_k \times B_k)
%   \]
%   by countable subadditivity of probability.
%   For $\mu_{\text{prod}}$, $\sigma$-subadditivity follows from the fact that it is an additive set function on the rectangle semiring induced by the finite measures $\Prob_X$ and $\Prob_Y$, hence a premeasure on $\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S}$. \cmark
%   % PATCH 4: Replace sigma-finite item with new text
%   \item 
%   Both are $\sigma$-finite: choose sets $S_1,S_2,\dots \in \mathcal{S}$ such that
%   \[
%   \R = \bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} S_n.
%   \]
%   Then
%   \[
%   \R^2 = \bigcup_{m,n=1}^{\infty} (S_m \times S_n),
%   \]
%   and for all $m,n \in \N$,
%   \[
%   \mu(S_m \times S_n) \le 1 < \infty
%   \qquad\text{and}\qquad
%   \mu_{\text{prod}}(S_m \times S_n) \le 1 < \infty.
%   \]
%   Hence both set functions are $\sigma$-finite. \cmark
% \end{itemize}

Therefore, the extensions $\tilde{\mu}, \tilde{\mu}_{\text{prod}}$ of ${\mu}, {\mu}_{\text{prod}}$ to $\sigma(\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S})$ are unique.



We \rlap{conclude}
\[
\Prob(X \in A, Y \in B)
=
\tilde{\mu}(A \times B)
=
\tilde{\mu}_{\text{prod}}(A \times B)
=
\Prob_X(A)\Prob_Y(B)
\]
for all $A, B \in \mathcal{B}(\R)$, because $A \times B \in \mathcal{B}(\R^2)=\sigma(\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S})$ and both extensions agree on all of $\sigma(\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S})$ by uniqueness. 
Hence $X$ and $Y$ are independent.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}\label{cor:independence-cdf}
Let $(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \Prob)$ be a probability space and let $X, Y : \Omega \to \R$ be random variables.
We define
\(
F_{X,Y}(x,y) \coloneq \Prob(X \le x, Y \le y)
\),
\(
F_X(x) \coloneq \Prob(X \le x)
\),
\(
F_Y(y) \coloneq \Prob(Y \le y)
\).
Then $X$ and $Y$ are independent if and only if
\[
F_{X,Y}(x,y) = F_X(x) F_Y(y)
\]
for all $x,y \in \R$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{example}[Independent Gaussian random variables]\label{ex:gaussian-random-variables}
It is well known that two zero-mean Gaussian random variables $X$ and $Y$ with zero correlation are independent.
Without loss of generality, let us assume that $X$ and $Y$ have zero mean.
By ``zero correlation'', we mean that $\Exp[XY] = 0$.
We have
\[
F_{X,Y}(x,y)
=
\int_{-\infty}^{x}\int_{-\infty}^{y} f_{X,Y}(x,y)\,\dif x\,\dif y
\]
with the density
\[
f_{X,Y}(x,y)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi\sigma_x\sigma_y}
\exp\left(
-\frac{x^2}{2\sigma_x^2}
-
\frac{y^2}{2\sigma_y^2}
\right)
\]
This density can be factorized into the product of two Gaussian densities
\[
f_{X,Y}(x,y)
=
\left(
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}\sigma_x}e^{-x^2/(2\sigma_x^2)}
\right)
\left(
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}\sigma_y}e^{-y^2/(2\sigma_y^2)}
\right)
\eqcolon
f_X(x)f_Y(y)
\]
Therefore, we have
\[
\begin{aligned}
F_{X,Y}(x,y)
&=
\int_{-\infty}^{x}\int_{-\infty}^{y} f_{X,Y}(x,y)\,\dif x\,\dif y
=
\int_{-\infty}^{x}\int_{-\infty}^{y} f_X(x)f_Y(y)\,\dif x\,\dif y
\\
&=
\int_{-\infty}^{x} f_X(x)\,\dif x
\int_{-\infty}^{y} f_Y(y)\,\dif y
=
F_X(x)F_Y(y)
\end{aligned}
\]
which shows by \autoref{cor:independence-cdf} that $X$ and $Y$ are independent.

While uncorrelatedness is necessary for independence, it is a much weaker condition in general.
When the joint probability distribution is not Gaussian, two random variables can be uncorrelated while still not being independent.
\end{example}

An important property of independence is that it is stable under measurable transformations.


\begin{theorem}[Independence under measurable transformations]\label{thm:independence-under-measurable-transformations}
% Let $(\Omega,\mathcal{F},\Prob)$ be a probability space and 
Let $X,Y : \Omega \to \R$ be independent random variables (\autoref{def:random-variable}).
Let $f,g : \R \to \R$ be $(\mathcal{B}(\R),\mathcal{B}(\R))$-measurable functions (\autoref{def:measurable-function}).
Then $f(X)$ and $g(Y)$ are independent random variables.
\end{theorem}
\begin{proof}
By \autoref{thm:composition-of-measurable-functions}, the functions $f(X)$ and $g(Y)$ are measurable, hence random variables.
To prove that they are independent, it suffices,
according to 
% \autoref{def:random-variable} and 
\autoref{def:independence-of-random-variables},
to show that for any two sets $A, \allowbreak B \allowbreak \in \allowbreak \mathcal{B}(\R)$, the events $\{f(X) \in A\}$ and $\{g(Y) \in B\}$ are independent.
% Here $A$ and $B$ are Borel subsets of $\R$, whereas $\{f(X)\in A\} = \{\omega\in\Omega : f(X(\omega))\in A\}$ and $\{g(Y)\in B\}$ are events in $\Omega$.

Since $f$ and $g$ are measurable, $f^{-1}(A)$ and $g^{-1}(B)$ are Borel sets.
We note that, by \autoref{fact:preimage-properties}.\ref{item:preimage-properties-composition},
\[
\{h(Z) \in C\} = (h \circ Z)^{-1}(C) = Z^{-1}(h^{-1}(C)) = \{Z \in h^{-1}(C)\} 
\]
and hence  $\{f(X) \in A\}=\{X\in f^{-1}(A)\}$ and $\{g(Y)\in B\}=\{Y\in g^{-1}(B)\}$.
By the independence of $X$ and $Y$, the events $\{X \in f^{-1}(A)\}$ and $\{Y \in g^{-1}(B)\}$ are independent.
Thus $\{f(X) \in A\}$ and $\{g(Y) \in B\}$ are independent.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}
\autoref{thm:independence-under-measurable-transformations} particularly applies to continuous and piecewise continuous functions and directly extends to random vectors.
\end{remark}

We close this section by considering independence of infinitely many random variables.


\begin{definition}\label{def:independent-family}
Let $(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \Prob)$ be a probability space and let $\mathcal{I}$ be an arbitrary index set.
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
  \item  \label{item:independent-family-events}
  A family of events $(A_i)_{i \in \mathcal{I}}$, where $A_i \in \mathcal{F}$ is \emph{independent} if
  \begin{equation}\label{eq:independent-family-events}
  \Prob\left(\bigcap_{i \in J} A_i\right) = \prod_{i \in J} \Prob(A_i)
  \end{equation}
  for all finite subsets $J \subseteq \mathcal{I}$.
  \item \label{item:independent-family-sets-of-events}
  A family $(\mathcal{A}_i)_{i \in \mathcal{I}}$, where $\mathcal{A}_i \subseteq \mathcal{F}$, is \emph{independent} if the family of events $(A_i)_{i \in \mathcal{I}}$ is independent for every choice of $A_i \in \mathcal{A}_i$. 
  \qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{definition}

\begin{remark}
An independent family of events $(A_i)_{i \in \mathcal{I}} \subseteq \mathcal{F}$ in particular is pairwise independent, i.e. $A_i \perp A_j$ for $i \neq j$.
The contrary is usually not true.
\end{remark}
\begin{example}
We roll a fair die twice and consider the following events:
\begin{itemize}
\item $A_1$: the first roll shows an odd number
\item $A_2$: the second roll shows an odd number
\item $A_3$: the sum of the dice is odd, i.e. $A_3 = A_1 \symdif A_2$ 
\end{itemize}

Then, $A_1 \perp A_2$, $A_1 \perp A_3$, $A_2 \perp A_3$, i.e. the events are pairwise independent.
However, 
\[
0 = \Prob(\varnothing) = \Prob(A_1 \cap A_2 \cap A_3) \neq \Prob(A_1)\Prob(A_2)\Prob(A_3)
\]
i.e. the family $A_1, A_2, A_3$ is not independent in the sense of \autoref{def:independent-family} \ref{item:independent-family-events}.
\end{example}

\begin{definition}
Let $(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \Prob)$ be a probability space and let $\mathcal{I}$ be an arbitrary index set.
The family $(X_i)_{i \in \mathcal{I}}$ of random variables is \emph{independent} if the family of $(\sigma(X_i))_{i \in \mathcal{I}}$ is independent.
\end{definition}

Finally, we remark that it is indeed sufficient to consider only finite intersections of events to define independence.
Let $(A_i)_{i \in \N}$ be a sequence of events such that any finite subfamily is independent, and let
\[
B_k \coloneq \bigcap_{i=1}^k A_i
\]
Then $(B_k)_{k \in \N}$ is a decreasing sequence with
\(
\bigcap_{i=1}^{\infty} A_i
=
\bigcap_{k=1}^{\infty} B_k
\).
By continuity of $\Prob$ from above (\autoref{thm:continuity-from-above}), we have
\[
\Prob\left(\bigcap_{i=1}^{\infty} A_i\right)
=
\Prob\left(\bigcap_{k=1}^{\infty} B_k\right)
=
\lim_{k \to \infty} \Prob(B_k)
\]
Since $A_1,\dots,A_k$ are independent, we obtain
\[
\Prob(B_k)
=
\Prob\left(\bigcap_{i=1}^k A_i\right)
=
\prod_{i=1}^k \Prob(A_i)
\]
and hence
\[
\Prob\left(\bigcap_{i=1}^{\infty} A_i\right)
=
\lim_{k \to \infty} \Prob(B_k)
=
\lim_{k \to \infty} \prod_{i=1}^k \Prob(A_i)
=
\prod_{i=1}^{\infty} \Prob(A_i)
\]



\subsection{Borel-Cantelli lemmas}



describe when events occur infinitely often.

\begin{theorem}\label{thm:first-borel-cantelli-lemma}
Let $(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \Prob)$ be a probability space and $(A_n)_{n \in \N}$, $A_n \in \mathcal{F}$, be a sequence of events.
Then
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
  \sum_{i=1}^{\infty} \Prob(A_i) < \infty
  \quad
  \Longrightarrow
  \quad
\Prob\left(\limsup_{i \to \infty} A_i\right) = 0
\end{verticalhack}
\qedhere
\]
\end{theorem}
\begin{proof}
We have
\[
\limsup_{i \to \infty} A_i
=
\bigcap_{k=1}^{\infty} \bigcup_{j=k}^{\infty} A_j
=
\bigcap_{k=1}^{\infty} B_k
\]
with $B_k \coloneq \bigcup_{j=k}^{\infty} A_j$ for $k \in \N$.
Since $(B_k)_{k \in \N}$ is a decreasing sequence of events, we have
\[
\Prob\left(\limsup_{i \to \infty} A_i\right)
=
\Prob\left(\bigcap_{k=1}^{\infty} B_k\right)
=
\lim_{k \to \infty} \Prob(B_k)
\]
by continuity of $\Prob$ from above (\autoref{thm:continuity-from-above}).
On the other hand, by the $\sigma$-subadditivity of $\Prob$ (\autoref{thm:countable-subadditivity}), we have
\[
\Prob(B_k)
=
\Prob\left(\bigcup_{\smash[b]{j}=k}^{\infty} A_j\right)
\leq
\sum_{j=k}^{\infty} \Prob(A_j)
\]
and since the series $\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} \Prob(A_i)$ converges, the tail $\sum_{j=k}^{\infty} \Prob(A_j)$ must go to zero as $k \to \infty$.
This implies $\Prob(B_k) \to 0$ as $k \to \infty$, which yields $\Prob\left(\limsup_{i \to \infty} A_i\right) = 0$.
\end{proof}



\begin{theorem}\label{thm:second-borel-cantelli-lemma}
Let $(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \Prob)$ be a probability space and $(A_n)_{n \in \N}$, $A_n \in \mathcal{F}$, be a sequence of events that are independent in the sense of \autoref{def:independent-family} \ref{item:independent-family-events}.
Then
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
  \sum_{i=1}^{\infty} \Prob(A_i) = \infty
  \quad
  \Longrightarrow
  \quad
\Prob\left(\limsup_{i \to \infty} A_i\right) = 1
\end{verticalhack}
\qedhere
\]
\end{theorem}
\begin{proof}
We consider 
\[
\left(\limsup_{i \to \infty} A_i\right)^{\complement}
=
\left(\bigcap_{k=1}^{\infty} \bigcup_{\smash[b]{j}=k}^{\infty} A_j\right)^\complement
\overset{\text{(\ref{eq:demorgan-intersection},\ref{eq:demorgan-union})}}{=}
\bigcup_{k=1}^{\infty} \bigcap_{\smash[b]{j}=k}^{\infty} A_j^\complement
\]
and show that 
\[
\Prob\left(\bigcup_{k=1}^{\infty} \bigcap_{\smash[b]{j}=k}^{\infty} A_j^\complement\right) = 0
\]
By \autoref{thm:countable-subadditivity}, it suffices to prove that $\Prob(\bigcap_{j=k}^{\infty} A_j^\complement) = 0$ for all $k \in \N$.

We use the inequality
$1 - x \leq \eu^{-x}$,
which holds for all $x \in \R$.
Fix $k \in \N$ and let $k < m \in \N$.
We have
\[
\Prob\left(\bigcap_{\smash[b]{j=k}}^{m} A_j^\complement\right)
\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:independent-family-events}}}{=}
\prod_{\smash[b]{j=k}}^{m} \Prob\left(A_j^\complement\right)
=
\prod_{\smash[b]{j=k}}^{m} (1 - \Prob(A_j))
\leq
\prod_{\smash[b]{j=k}}^{m} \eu^{-\Prob(A_j)}
=
\eu^{-\sum_{j=k}^{m} \Prob(A_j)}
\]
where we used the fact that independence is preserved under taking complements.

Since $\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} \Prob(A_i) = \infty$, the tail $\sum_{j=k}^{m} \Prob(A_j)$ must go to infinity as $m \to \infty$
and hence 
\[
\eu^{-\sum_{j=k}^{m} \Prob(A_j)} \to 0
\]
as $m \to \infty$.
By continuity of $\Prob$ from above (\autoref{thm:continuity-from-above}), we have
\[
\Prob\left(\bigcap_{\smash[b]{j=k}}^{\infty} A_j^\complement\right)
=
\lim_{m \to \infty} \Prob\left(\bigcap_{\smash[b]{j=k}}^{m} A_j^\complement\right)
\leq
\lim_{m \to \infty} \eu^{-\sum_{j=k}^{m} \Prob(A_j)}
=
0
\]
for all $k \in \N$.
\end{proof}

\definecolor{monkey8A8A8A}{HTML}{8A8A8A}
\definecolor{monkey848283}{HTML}{848283}
\definecolor{monkey525252}{HTML}{525252}
\definecolor{monkey7B797A}{HTML}{7B797A}
\definecolor{monkey4C4C4C}{HTML}{4C4C4C}
\definecolor{monkey8D8C8C}{HTML}{8D8C8C}
\definecolor{monkey616060}{HTML}{616060}
\definecolor{monkey525052}{HTML}{525052}
\definecolor{monkey706F70}{HTML}{706F70}
\definecolor{monkey636262}{HTML}{636262}
\definecolor{monkey808080}{HTML}{808080}
Let $\mathcal{A}$ be a finite alphabet and $w = a_1 \cdots a_n$ a fixed word of length $n$ with $a_i \in \mathcal{A}$ for all $i$.
Consider an infinite random string $(X_i)_{i \in \N}$ where the $X_i$ are independent and uniformly distributed on $\mathcal{A}$.
% Then, with probability $1$, the word $w$ appears infinitely often in the infinite string in $n$ consecutive positions.
Then, with probability $1$, the word $w$ appears infinitely often as a consecutive block in the infinite string.

To see this, partition the infinite string into infinitely many disjoint blocks of length $n$.
% The first block consists of positions $1$ to $n$, the second of positions $n+1$ to $2n$ and so on.
For $k \in \N$, let $A_k$ be the event that the $k$-th block (i.e. the positions $(k-1)n+1,\ldots,kn$) is exactly equal to the word $w$.
Since the blocks do not overlap, the events $(A_k)_{k \ge 1}$ are independent.
Moreover,
\[
\Prob(A_k)
=
\prod_{i=1}^{n} \Prob(X_{(k-1)n+i}=a_i)
=
\left(\frac{1}{|\mathcal{A}|}\right)^n
>
0
\]%
for every $k \in \N$.
Hence\tikzmark{monkeyTypingEquationAnchor}
\[
\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \Prob(A_k)
=
\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \left(\frac{1}{|\mathcal{A}|}\right)^n
=
\infty
\]
By \autoref{thm:second-borel-cantelli-lemma}, this implies
\[
\Prob\left(\limsup_{k \to \infty} A_k\right)=1
\]
Thus infinitely many of the blocks are equal to $w$ with probability $1$.
In particular, the word $w$ appears at least once in the infinite string in $n$ consecutive positions with probability $1$.

\path[fill=monkey6E6D6D,even odd rule] svg{M448.169 100.828L449.052 100.092C449.97 99.8412 450.413 100.143 451.317 100.503C451.108 100.851 450.774 101.359 450.621 101.715C449.852 101.511 448.909 101.129 448.169 100.828Z};

\path[fill=monkeyA4A3A4,even odd rule] svg{M171.716 17.539C172.664 17.6363 173.104 17.5941 173.314 18.596C172.99 19.4546 172.536 19.7224 171.836 20.32L171.071 20.1549C170.014 18.9453 170.67 18.3506 171.716 17.539Z}; 
\path[fill=monkeyA4A3A4,even odd rule] svg{M200.755 149.124C201.132 148.816 201.688 148.872 201.995 149.25C202.303 149.627 202.246 150.183 201.868 150.49C201.491 150.797 200.936 150.74 200.629 150.363C200.322 149.987 200.378 149.432 200.755 149.124Z}; 
\path[fill=monkeyD8D8D8,even odd rule] svg{M468.638 172.911C469.029 172.744 469.483 172.91 469.673 173.291C469.864 173.671 469.726 174.134 469.357 174.347C469.099 174.497 468.778 174.491 468.525 174.332C468.272 174.172 468.128 173.886 468.152 173.587C468.175 173.289 468.363 173.029 468.638 172.911Z}; 
\path[fill=monkey888788,even odd rule] svg{M468.645 375.885L469.374 376.221L469.344 376.625L468.321 377.373C467.841 377.121 467.81 377.077 467.373 376.75L467.489 376.367L468.645 375.885Z};
  \end{tikzpicture}%
  \tikzexternaldisable
  };
\end{tikzpicture}%

Let $w$ be the complete works of Shakespeare and consider a monkey randomly typing on a typewriter one character at a time.
\autoref{thm:second-borel-cantelli-lemma} then states that, given infinite time, the monkey will reproduce the works of Shakespeare infinitely often with probability $1$.
\end{example}
\tikzexternalenable




Combining \autoref{thm:first-borel-cantelli-lemma} and \autoref{thm:second-borel-cantelli-lemma} yields Kolmogorov's zero-one law for independent events.
\begin{theorem}
Let $(\Omega,\mathcal{F},\Prob)$ be a probability space and let $(A_n)_{n \in \N}$ be a sequence of independent events.
Then
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
\Prob\left(\limsup_{i \to \infty} A_i\right) 
=
\begin{cases}
1 & \text{if } \sum_{i=1}^{\infty} \Prob(A_i) = \infty \\
0 & \text{if } \sum_{i=1}^{\infty} \Prob(A_i) < \infty
\end{cases}
\end{verticalhack}
\qedhere
\]
\end{theorem}


Similarly to \autoref{def:sigma-algebra-generated-by-random-variable}, we can define the $\sigma$-algebra generated by a collection of random variables:
\begin{definition}\label{def:sigma-algebra-generated-by-collection-of-random-variables}
Let $(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \Prob)$ be a probability space and let $\{X_i : i \in I\}$ be a collection of random variables, where $I$ is an arbitrary, possibly countably or uncountably infinite index set.
We call
\[
{\color{red}\boxed{\color{black}
\sigma(\{X_i : i \in I\}) \coloneq \sigma\bigl(\{X_i^{-1}(B) : i \in I, B \in \mathcal{B}(\R)\}\bigr) \subseteq \mathcal{F}
}}
\]
the \hl[2]{\emph{$\sigma$-algebra generated by $\{X_i : i \in I\}$}}, sometimes abbreviated $\sigma(X_i : i \in I)$.
\end{definition}

\begin{remark}
Unlike for single random variables (\autoref{def:sigma-algebra-generated-by-random-variable}), the outer $\sigma(\cdot)$ is necessary here because the collection
\[
\{X_i^{-1}(B) : i \in I,\ B \in \mathcal{B}(\R)\}
\]
is generally not a $\sigma$-algebra itself: 
it contains events depending on one random variable at a time, but not necessarily events involving several random variables simultaneously.
% such as
% \[
% \{X_i \in B_i\} \cap \{X_j \in B_j\}
% \]
% for $i \neq j$.
In contrast, for a single random variable $X$, the collection
\[
\{X^{-1}(B) : B \in \mathcal{B}(\R)\}
\]
is already a $\sigma$-algebra, since, by \autoref{fact:preimage-properties}, preimages preserve the set operations required for a $\sigma$-algebra (\autoref{def:sigma-algebra}).
\end{remark}


\clearpage


\section{The integral}

\subsection{Construction of the integral}

The construction a general measure integral is performed in the following steps:
\begin{enumerate}
\item \autoref{def:integral-of-indicator-function}: Construction for indicator functions \label{step:integral-of-indicator-function}
\item \autoref{def:integral-of-nonnegative-simple-function}: Construction for non-negative simple functions \label{step:integral-of-nonnegative-simple-function}
\item \autoref{def:integral-of-nonnegative-measurable-function}: Construction for non-negative measurable functions \label{step:integral-of-nonnegative-measurable-function}
\item \autoref{def:integral-of-integrable-function}: Construction for integrable functions \label{step:integral-of-integrable-function}
\end{enumerate}

\medskip

Throughout this section, let $(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \mu)$ be a measure space.

\begin{definition}[Step \ref{step:integral-of-indicator-function}]\label{def:integral-of-indicator-function}
Let $f \coloneq \ind_A$ with $A \in \mathcal{F}$. 
We define
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
\int f\,\dif\mu \coloneq \mu(A)
\end{verticalhack}
\qedhere
\]
\end{definition}

Next, we consider non-negative simple functions
\begin{equation}\label{eq:non-negative-simple-function}
f \coloneq \sum_{i=1}^m \alpha_i \ind_{A_i}
\end{equation}
where $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_m \geq 0$ and $A_1, \ldots, A_m \in \mathcal{F}$ with $\Omega = \bigcup_{i=1}^m A_i$.
Without loss of generality, we assume $A_i \cap A_j = \varnothing$ for $i \neq j$.
As the representation of $f$ in \eqref{eq:non-negative-simple-function} is not unique, we require the following lemma, to be able to define an integral in a sensible way.



% --- BEGIN PATCHED BLOCK ---
\begin{lemma}\label{lem:integral-of-simple-function-well-defined}
Let $f$ be a non-negative simple function and suppose
\[
f
=
\sum_{i=1}^m \alpha_i \ind_{A_i}
=
\sum_{j=1}^n \beta_j \ind_{B_j}
\]
Then, there holds
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
\sum_{i=1}^m \alpha_i \mu(A_i)
=
\sum_{j=1}^n \beta_j \mu(B_j)
\end{verticalhack}
\qedhere
\]
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
By definition of $f$, there holds
\[
\mu(A_i)
=
\sum_{j=1}^n \mu(A_i \cap B_j)
\qquad
% \text{for }
 i = 1,\ldots,m
\]
as well as
\[
\mu(B_j)
=
\sum_{i=1}^m \mu(A_i \cap B_j)
\qquad
% \text{for }
 j = 1,\ldots,n
\]
Furthermore, we observe that $\alpha_i = \beta_j$, whenever $\mu(A_i \cap B_j) > 0$.
Hence, we immediately obtain
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
\sum_{i=1}^m \alpha_i\mu(A_i)
=
\sum_{i=1}^m
\sum_{j=1}^n 
\alpha_i \mu(A_i \cap B_j)
=
\sum_{j=1}^n 
\sum_{i=1}^m
\beta_j \mu(A_i \cap B_j)
=
\sum_{j=1}^n \beta_j\mu(B_j)
\end{verticalhack}
\qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[Step \ref{step:integral-of-nonnegative-simple-function}]\label{def:integral-of-nonnegative-simple-function}
Let $f = \sum_{i=1}^m \alpha_i\ind_{A_i}$ be a non-negative simple function.
We define
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
\int f\,\dif\mu
\coloneq
\sum_{i=1}^m \alpha_i\mu(A_i)
\end{verticalhack}
\qedhere
\]
\end{definition}

The integral derived in the previous definition has the following elementary properties.

\begin{lemma}\label{lem:elementary-properties-integral-simple-functions}
Let $f,g$ be non-negative simple functions and let $a \in [0,\infty)$.
There holds
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item 
\label{lem:elementary-properties-integral-simple-functions:additivity}
$\int (f+g)\,\dif\mu = \int f\,\dif\mu + \int g\,\dif\mu$
\item 
\label{lem:elementary-properties-integral-simple-functions:homogeneity}
$\int (af)\,\dif\mu = a\int f\,\dif\mu$
\item 
\label{lem:elementary-properties-integral-simple-functions:monotonicity}
$f \leq g$ (pointwise) implies $\int f\,\dif\mu \leq \int g\,\dif\mu$
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item  \label{proof:elementary-properties-integral-simple-functions:additivity}
Let $f = \sum_{i=1}^m \alpha_i\ind_{A_i}$, $g = \sum_{j=1}^n \beta_j\ind_{B_j}$.
Since $A_1,\ldots,A_m$ as well as $B_1,\ldots,B_n$ are disjoint partitions of $\Omega$, we can write
\[
f
=
\sum_{i=1}^m\sum_{j=1}^n \alpha_i\ind_{A_i\cap B_j}
\qquad
 g
=
\sum_{i=1}^m\sum_{j=1}^n \beta_j\ind_{A_i\cap B_j}
\]
Hence, we have the representation
\[
f+g
=
\sum_{i=1}^m\sum_{j=1}^n (\alpha_i+\beta_j)\ind_{A_i\cap B_j}
\]
implying
\begingroup
{\fontsize{6}{10}\selectfont
\[
% \begin{aligned}
\int (f+g)\,\dif\mu
% &=
=
\sum_{i=1}^m\sum_{j=1}^n (\alpha_i+\beta_j)\mu(A_i \cap B_j) 
% \\
% &=
=
\sum_{i=1}^m\sum_{j=1}^n \alpha_i\mu(A_i \cap B_j)
+
\sum_{i=1}^m\sum_{j=1}^n \beta_j\mu(A_i \cap B_j) 
% \\
% &=
=
\int f\,\dif\mu + \int g\,\dif\mu
% \end{aligned}
\]
}
\endgroup

\item Follows directly from the definition of the integral and the linearity of the sum.
Indeed, if $f = \sum_{i=1}^m \alpha_i\ind_{A_i}$, then $af = a\sum_{i=1}^m \alpha_i\ind_{A_i} = \sum_{i=1}^m a\alpha_i\ind_{A_i}$, and hence
\[
\int af\,\dif\mu
= % why?
\sum_{i=1}^m a\alpha_i\mu(A_i)
=
a\sum_{i=1}^m \alpha_i\mu(A_i)
=
a\int f\,\dif\mu
\]

\item Let $f,g$ be represented as in \ref{proof:elementary-properties-integral-simple-functions:additivity}.
Then, there holds $\alpha_i \leq \beta_j$ whenever $\mu(A_i \cap B_j) > 0$.
We obtain
\[
\int f\,\dif\mu
=
\sum_{i=1}^m\sum_{j=1}^n \alpha_i\mu(A_i \cap B_j)
\leq
\sum_{i=1}^m\sum_{j=1}^n \beta_j\mu(A_i \cap B_j)
=
\int g\,\dif\mu
\]
as claimed.
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{proof}

To define the integral for non-negative measurable functions, we require the following approximation property.

\begin{lemma}\label{lem:approximation-by-simple-functions}
Let $f : \Omega \to [0,\infty]$ be measurable (\autoref{def:measurable-function}).
Then %the following statements hold.
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item 
there exists a monotonically increasing sequence $(f_n)_{n \in \N}$ of non-negative simple functions such that $f_n \to f$
\item 
there exist measurable sets $A_1,A_2,\ldots \in \mathcal{F}$ and numbers $\alpha_1,\alpha_2,\ldots \geq 0$ such that
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
f
=
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \alpha_n\ind_{A_n}
\end{verticalhack}
\qedhere
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}
\[
\begin{tikzpicture}[every node/.style={font=\footnotesize}]
\definecolor{functioncol}{HTML}{\eat #00a8c6}
\definecolor{approximcol}{HTML}{\eat #399B00}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\domainRadius}{2}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\functionMaximum}{\domainRadius*\domainRadius}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\xScale}{2.25}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\yScale}{1.5}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\xAxisPadding}{0.35}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\yAxisPadding}{0.35}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\xAxisMin}{-\xScale*\domainRadius}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\xAxisMax}{\xScale*\domainRadius}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\yAxisMax}{\yScale*\functionMaximum}
% 
% Coordinate conversion: mathematical point (x,y) -> TikZ point.
\newcommand{\pt}[2]{({\xScale*(#1)},{\yScale*(#2)})}
% 
% Axes.
\draw[->] (\xAxisMin,0) -- (\xAxisMax+\xAxisPadding,0) node[right] {$x$};
\draw[->] (\xAxisMin,0) -- (\xAxisMin,\yAxisMax+\yAxisPadding);
% 
% Ticks.
% \foreach \x in {-2,-1.5,-1,-0.5,0,0.5,1,1.5,2}{
\foreach \x in {-2,-1,0,1,2}{
  \draw \pt{\x}{0.04} -- \pt{\x}{-0.04} node[below] {$\x$};
}
\foreach \y in {0,1,2,3,4}{
  \draw (\xAxisMin-0.06,{\yScale*\y}) -- (\xAxisMin+0.06,{\yScale*\y}) node[left=2pt] {$\y$};
}
% 
% Draw f(x)=x^2.
\draw[functioncol, thick, domain=-\domainRadius:\domainRadius, samples=160, variable=\x]
  plot ({\xScale*\x},{\yScale*(\x*\x)});

\newcount\approximationStep
% 
% Draw the exact simple approximation
%   f_n(x) = min{2^{-n} floor(2^n x^2), n}
% for f(x)=x^2, with vertical jump connections.
\newcommand{\DrawSimpleApprox}[2]{%
  \pgfmathtruncatemacro{\twoToN}{2^#1}%
  \pgfmathtruncatemacro{\lastStep}{#1*\twoToN-1}%
  \ifnum#1=0
    \draw[#2] \pt{-\domainRadius}{0} -- \pt{\domainRadius}{0};%
  \else
    \pgfmathsetmacro{\cap}{sqrt(#1)}%
    \draw[#2, line join=miter, line cap=butt]
      \pt{-\domainRadius}{#1} -- \pt{-\cap}{#1}
      foreach \approximationStep in {\lastStep,...,0} {
        -- \pt{-sqrt((\approximationStep+1)/\twoToN)}{\approximationStep/\twoToN}
        -- \pt{-sqrt(\approximationStep/\twoToN)}{\approximationStep/\twoToN}
      }
      foreach \approximationStep in {0,...,\lastStep} {
        -- \pt{sqrt((\approximationStep+1)/\twoToN)}{\approximationStep/\twoToN}
        -- \pt{sqrt((\approximationStep+1)/\twoToN)}{(\approximationStep+1)/\twoToN}
      }
      -- \pt{\domainRadius}{#1};%
  \fi
}
% 
\DrawSimpleApprox{0}{approximcol, thick, opacity=0.5}
\DrawSimpleApprox{1}{approximcol, thick, opacity=0.5}
\DrawSimpleApprox{2}{approximcol, thick, opacity=0.5}
\DrawSimpleApprox{3}{approximcol, thick, opacity=0.5}
% 
% Labels.
\node[anchor=south, approximcol, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=2pt] at \pt{1.875}{0} {$f_0$};
\node[anchor=south, approximcol, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=2pt] at \pt{1.875}{1} {$f_1$};
\node[anchor=south, approximcol, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=2pt] at \pt{1.875}{2} {$f_2$};
\node[anchor=south, approximcol, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=2pt] at \pt{1.875}{3} {$f_3$};
\node[anchor=south west, functioncol, inner sep=0pt, outer sep=2pt] at \pt{-1.875}{3.515625} {$f(x)=x^2$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\]



\begin{proof}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item \label{proof:approximation-by-simple-functions:monotonically-increasing-sequence-of-simple-functions}
For $n \in \N_0$, define
\(
f_n = \min\left\{2^{-n}\lfloor 2^n f \rfloor, n\right\}
\).
The function $f_n$ is measurable % why?
 and assumes at most $n2^n+1$ (finitely many) different values; $0, 2^{-n}, 2\cdot 2^{-n}, 3\cdot 2^{-n}, \ldots, n$.
Hence it is a simple function.
Clearly, $f_n(\omega) \leq f(\omega)$ for all $\omega \in \Omega$.
Moreover, the sequence $f_n$ is monotonically increasing and converging to~$f$.



% \item 
% Let $f_n$ be as in \ref{proof:approximation-by-simple-functions:monotonically-increasing-sequence-of-simple-functions} and set
% \[
% B_{n,i} \coloneq \{\omega \in \Omega : f_n(\omega)-f_{n-1}(\omega) = i2^{-n}\}
% \qquad
% \text{and}
% \qquad
% \beta_{n,i} \coloneq i2^{-n}
% \]
% for $n \in \N$ and $i = 1,\ldots,2^n$.
% Hence
% \[
% f_n-f_{n-1}
% =
% \sum_{i=1}^{2^n} \beta_{n,i}\ind_{B_{n,i}}
% \]
% By changing the numeration $(n,i) \mapsto m$, we get $(\alpha_m)_{m \in \N}$ and $(A_m)_{m \in \N}$ such that
% \[
% \begin{verticalhack}
% f
% \overset{\eqref{eq:telescopic-identity}}{=}
% f_0
% +
% \sum_{n=1}^{\infty}(f_n-f_{n-1})
% =
% \sum_{n=1}^{\infty}\sum_{i=1}^{2^n} \beta_{n,i}\ind_{B_{n,i}}
% =
% \sum_{m=1}^{\infty}\alpha_m\ind_{A_m}
% \end{verticalhack}
% \qedhere
% \]
\item 
Let $f_n$ be as in \ref{proof:approximation-by-simple-functions:monotonically-increasing-sequence-of-simple-functions}.
Since the sequence $(f_n)_{n \in \N_0}$ is monotonically increasing, the functions $f_n-f_{n-1}$ are non-negative for every $n \in \N$.
Moreover, since $f_n$ and $f_{n-1}$ are simple functions, also $f_n-f_{n-1}$ is a non-negative simple function.
Set
\[
B_{n,i} \coloneq \{\omega \in \Omega : f_n(\omega)-f_{n-1}(\omega) = i2^{-n}\}
\qquad
\text{and}
\qquad
\beta_{n,i} \coloneq i2^{-n}
\]
for $n \in \N$ and $i = 1,\ldots,2^n$.
Since $f_n-f_{n-1}$ is measurable, all sets $B_{n,i}$ are measurable. % why? do we have a theorem?
Furthermore, the sets $B_{n,i}$ describe the non-zero level sets of $f_n-f_{n-1}$.
% This is compatible with the fact that $f_n$ itself assumes at most $n2^n+1$ different values: here we do not describe the level sets of $f_n$, but the level sets of the increment $f_n-f_{n-1}$.
For every fixed $n \in \N$, the refinement from $f_{n-1}$ to $f_n$ can increase the value by at most $1$, and the possible increments are multiples of $2^{-n}$. % why?
So the possible non-zero values of $f_n-f_{n-1}$ are contained in
\(
2^{-n},2\cdot 2^{-n}, 3\cdot 2^{-n},\ldots,1
\).
The zero level is omitted, since it does not con\-tri\-bute to the sum.
Hence
\[
f_n-f_{n-1}
=
\sum_{i=1}^{2^n} \beta_{n,i}\ind_{B_{n,i}}
\]
for every $n \in \N$.
% Since $f_n \to f$, the telescopic identity gives pointwise
% \[
% f
% \overset{\eqref{eq:telescopic-identity}}{=}
% f_0
% + 
% \sum_{n=1}^{\infty}(f_n-f_{n-1})
% \]
% By changing the numeration $(n,i) \mapsto m$, we get $(\alpha_m)_{m \in \N}$ and $(A_m)_{m \in \N}$ such that
% \[
% f
% =
% f_0
% + 
% \sum_{n=1}^{\infty}(f_n-f_{n-1})
% =
% \sum_{m=1}^{\infty}\alpha_m\ind_{A_m}
% \]
% Here, the term $f_0$ is included in the renumeration as well.
By changing the numeration $(n,i) \mapsto m$, we get $(\alpha_m)_{m \in \N}$ and $(A_m)_{m \in \N}$ such \rlap{that}
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
f
\overset{\eqref{eq:telescopic-identity}}{=}
f_0
+
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty}(f_n-f_{n-1})
=
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty}\sum_{i=1}^{2^n} \beta_{n,i}\ind_{B_{n,i}}
=
\sum_{m=1}^{\infty}\alpha_m\ind_{A_m}
\end{verticalhack}
\qedhere
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{proof}

Similarly to the case of simple functions (\autoref{lem:integral-of-simple-function-well-defined}), we need to show that the integral is independent of the approximating sequence.

\begin{lemma}\label{lem:integral-independent-of-approximating-sequence}
Let $(u_n)_{n \in \N}$ and $(v_m)_{m \in \N}$ be monotonically increasing sequences of non-negative simple functions.
Then, if $\sup_{n \in \N}u_n = \sup_{m \in \N}v_m$, there holds
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
\sup_{n \in \N}\int u_n\,\dif\mu
=
\sup_{m \in \N}\int v_m\,\dif\mu
\end{verticalhack}
\qedhere
\]
\end{lemma}

% \begin{proof}
% Let $v_m = \sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\ind_{A_{m,j}}$ and define the sets $B_{n,m}^s \coloneq \{u_n \geq sv_m\} \in \mathcal{F}$, $s \in \R$.
% For $s \in (0,1)$, we have due to $\sup_{n \in \N}u_n \geq v_m$ that
% \[
% \bigcup_{n \in \N} B_{n,m}^s
% =
% \Omega
% \]
% and, consequently,
% \[
% A_{m,j}
% \cap
% \bigcup_{n \in \N} B_{n,m}^s
% =
% A_{m,j}
% \]
% By construction, we have $u_n \geq sv_m\ind_{B_{n,m}^s}$, where the latter is a non-negative simple function.
% This implies
% \[
% \int u_n\,\dif\mu
% \geq
% s\int v_m\ind_{B_{n,m}^s}\,\dif\mu
% =
% s\sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\mu(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s)
% \]
% and we infer for the supremum
% \[
% \sup_{n \in \N}\int u_n\,\dif\mu
% \geq
% s\sup_{n \in \N}\sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\mu(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s)
% =
% s\sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\mu(A_{m,j})
% =
% s\int v_m\,\dif\mu
% \]
% Herein, we have used that $B_{n,m}^s$ forms an increasing sequence as well as the continuity of $\mu$ from below, see \autoref{thm:continuity-from-below}.
% For $s \to 1$, we obtain
% \[
% \sup_{n \in \N}\int u_n\,\dif\mu
% \geq
% \sup_{m \in \N}\int v_m\,\dif\mu
% \]
% The opposite inequality is obtained analogously.
% \end{proof}


% \begin{proof}
% % Fix $m \in \N$ and write
% Let
% \(
% v_m = \sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\ind_{A_{m,j}}
% \) for $m \in \N$.
% Define
% \[
% B_{n,m}^s \coloneq \{\omega \in \Omega : u_n(\omega) \geq sv_m(\omega)\}
% \]
% with $s \in \R$.
% Since $u_n$ and $v_m$ are measurable, we have $B_{n,m}^s \in \mathcal{F}$.
% Moreover, since $(u_n)_{n \in \N}$ is monotonically increasing, the sets $B_{n,m}^s$ are monotonically increasing in $n$ as well.
% %  i.e.
% % \[
% % B_{1,m}^s \subseteq B_{2,m}^s \subseteq B_{3,m}^s \subseteq \cdots
% % \]
% Indeed, if $\omega \in B_{n,m}^s$, then $u_n(\omega) \geq sv_m(\omega)$ and, since $u_{n+1}(\omega) \geq u_n(\omega)$, we get $u_{n+1}(\omega) \geq sv_m(\omega)$, hence $\omega \in B_{n+1,m}^s$.

% For $s \in (0,1)$, we have due to $\sup_{n \in \N}u_n \geq v_m$ that
% \[
% \bigcup_{n = 1}^{\infty} B_{n,m}^s
% =
% \Omega
% \]
% Indeed, if $\omega \in \Omega$ and $v_m(\omega)=0$, then $u_n(\omega) \geq 0 = sv_m(\omega)$ for every $n \in \N$.
% If $v_m(\omega)>0$, then
% \[
% \sup_{n \in \N}u_n(\omega)
% \geq
% v_m(\omega)
% >
% sv_m(\omega)
% \]
% and hence there exists $n \in \N$ such that $u_n(\omega) \geq sv_m(\omega)$.
% Thus, in both cases, $\omega \in B_{n,m}^s$ for some $n \in \N$.
% Consequently,
% \[
% A_{m,j}
% \cap
% \bigcup_{n = 1}^{\infty} B_{n,m}^s
% =
% A_{m,j}
% \]
% for every $j=1,\ldots,k_m$, and therefore, since $A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s$ is increasing in $n$, 
% continuity from below (\autoref{thm:continuity-from-below}) gives
% \[
% \lim_{n \to \infty}\mu(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s)
% =
% \mu\left(\bigcup_{n = 1}^{\infty}(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s)\right)
% =
% \mu\left(A_{m,j}\cap\bigcup_{n = 1}^{\infty}B_{n,m}^s\right)
% =
% \mu(A_{m,j})
% \]

% By construction, we have $u_n \geq sv_m\ind_{B_{n,m}^s}$, where the latter is a non-negative simple function.
% This implies
% \[
% \int u_n\,\dif\mu
% \geq
% s\int v_m\ind_{B_{n,m}^s}\,\dif\mu
% =
% s\sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\mu(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s)
% \]
% and we infer for the supremum
% \[
% \begin{aligned}
% \sup_{n \in \N}\int u_n\,\dif\mu
% &\geq
% s\sup_{n \in \N}\sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\mu(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s) \\
% &=
% s\lim_{n \to \infty}\sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\mu(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s) \\
% &=
% s\sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\lim_{n \to \infty}\mu(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s) \\
% &=
% s\sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\mu(A_{m,j}) \\
% &=
% s\int v_m\,\dif\mu
% \end{aligned}
% \]
% Herein, we have used that $B_{n,m}^s$ forms an increasing sequence as well as the continuity of $\mu$ from below, see \autoref{thm:continuity-from-below}.
% For $s \to 1$, we obtain
% \[
% \sup_{n \in \N}\int u_n\,\dif\mu
% \geq
% \int v_m\,\dif\mu
% \]
% Since $m \in \N$ was arbitrary, we get
% \[
% \sup_{n \in \N}\int u_n\,\dif\mu
% \geq
% \sup_{m \in \N}\int v_m\,\dif\mu
% \]
% The opposite inequality is obtained analogously.
% \end{proof}


\begin{proof}
% Fix $m \in \N$ and write
% \[
% v_m = \sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\ind_{A_{m,j}}
% \]
% Let $s \in (0,1)$ and define
% \[
% B_{n,m}^s \coloneq \{\omega \in \Omega : u_n(\omega) \geq sv_m(\omega)\}
% \]
Let $v_m = \sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\ind_{A_{m,j}}$ and define the sets $B_{n,m}^s \coloneq \{u_n \geq sv_m\} \in \mathcal{F}$, $s \in \R$.
Since $u_n$ and $v_m$ are measurable, we have $B_{n,m}^s \in \mathcal{F}$.
Moreover, since $(u_n)_{n \in \N}$ is monotonically increasing, the sets $B_{n,m}^s$ are monotonically increasing in $n$ as well.
% , i.e.
% \[
% B_{1,m}^s \subseteq B_{2,m}^s \subseteq B_{3,m}^s \subseteq \cdots
% \]
Indeed, if $\omega \in B_{n,m}^s$, then $u_n(\omega) \geq sv_m(\omega)$ and $u_{n+1}(\omega) \geq u_n(\omega)$, hence $\omega \in B_{n+1,m}^s$.

% We claim that
% \[
% \bigcup_{n \in \N} B_{n,m}^s
% =
% \Omega
% \]
% Let $\omega \in \Omega$.
% If $v_m(\omega)=0$, then $u_n(\omega) \geq 0 = sv_m(\omega)$ for every $n \in \N$, hence $\omega \in B_{n,m}^s$ for every $n \in \N$.
% If $v_m(\omega)>0$, then, since $\sup_{n \in \N}u_n(\omega) = \sup_{\ell \in \N}v_\ell(\omega) \geq v_m(\omega)$ and $s<1$, there exists some $n \in \N$ such that
% \[
% u_n(\omega) \geq sv_m(\omega)
% \]
% Otherwise, $u_n(\omega) < sv_m(\omega)$ for all $n \in \N$, and therefore
% \[
% \sup_{n \in \N}u_n(\omega) \leq sv_m(\omega) < v_m(\omega),
% \]
% which contradicts $\sup_{n \in \N}u_n(\omega) \geq v_m(\omega)$.
% This proves the claim.

For $s \in (0,1)$, we have due to $\sup_{n \in \N}u_n \geq v_m$ that
\(
\bigcup_{n = 1}^{\infty} B_{n,m}^s
=
\Omega
\).
Consequently, for every $j=1,\ldots,k_m$, we have
\[
A_{m,j}
\cap
\bigcup_{n = 1}^{\infty} B_{n,m}^s
=
A_{m,j}
\]
and therefore, since $A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s$ is increasing in $n$, continuity from below (\autoref{thm:continuity-from-below}) gives
% \[
% \lim_{n \to \infty}\mu(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s)
% =
% \mu\left(A_{m,j}\cap\bigcup_{n \in \N}B_{n,m}^s\right)
% =
% \mu(A_{m,j})
% \]
\begin{equation}\label{proof:integral-independent-of-approximating-sequence:continuity-from-below-application}
\lim_{n \to \infty}\mu(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s)
\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:continuity-increasing}}}{=}
\mu\left(\bigcup_{n = 1}^{\infty}(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s)\right)
=
\mu\left(A_{m,j}\cap\bigcup_{n = 1}^{\infty}B_{n,m}^s\right)
=
\mu(A_{m,j})
\end{equation}

By construction, we have
\(
u_n \geq sv_m\ind_{B_{n,m}^s}
\),
since, on $B_{n,m}^s$ this is exactly the inequality $u_n \geq sv_m$, and outside $B_{n,m}^s$ the right hand side is zero.
Moreover, $sv_m\ind_{B_{n,m}^s}$ is a non-negative simple function \eqref{eq:non-negative-simple-function}, \rlap{since}
\[
sv_m\ind_{B_{n,m}^s}
% =
% s \left(\sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\ind_{A_{m,j}}\right)\ind_{B_{n,m}^s}
=
s \sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\ind_{A_{m,j}} \ind_{B_{n,m}^s}
=
\sum_{j=1}^{k_m}s\alpha_{m,j}\ind_{A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s}
\]
Hence, by \autoref{lem:elementary-properties-integral-simple-functions},
\[
\int u_n\,\dif\mu
\overset{\text{\ref{lem:elementary-properties-integral-simple-functions:monotonicity}}}{\geq}
\int s v_m\ind_{B_{n,m}^s}\,\dif\mu
\overset{\text{\ref{lem:elementary-properties-integral-simple-functions:homogeneity}}}{=}
s\int v_m\ind_{B_{n,m}^s}\,\dif\mu
\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:non-negative-simple-function}}}{=}
s\sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\mu(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s)
\]
Taking the supremum over $n$ yields
\[
\begin{aligned}
\sup_{n \in \N}\int u_n\,\dif\mu
&\geq
s\sup_{n \in \N}\sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\mu(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s)
=
s \lim_{n \to \infty}\sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\mu(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s) \\
&=
s \sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\lim_{n \to \infty}\mu(A_{m,j}\cap B_{n,m}^s) 
\overset{\text{\eqref{proof:integral-independent-of-approximating-sequence:continuity-from-below-application}}}{=}
s \sum_{j=1}^{k_m}\alpha_{m,j}\mu(A_{m,j}) 
=
s \int v_m\,\dif\mu
\end{aligned}
\]
where we used the fact that  the sequence inside the supremum is increasing in $n$ (hence the limit and the supremum are equal), 
and since the sum is finite, we could pass the limit through the sum.
% Therefore,
% \[
% \sup_{n \in \N}\int u_n\,\dif\mu
% \geq
% s\int v_m\,\dif\mu
% \]
% Letting $s \to 1$ gives
% \[
% \sup_{n \in \N}\int u_n\,\dif\mu
% \geq
% \int v_m\,\dif\mu
% \]
% Since $m \in \N$ was arbitrary, we get
% \[
% \sup_{n \in \N}\int u_n\,\dif\mu
% \geq
% \sup_{m \in \N}\int v_m\,\dif\mu
% \]
Letting $s \nearrow 1$ and taking the supremum over $m \in \N$ yields the claim.
The opposite inequality is obtained analogously, by exchanging the roles of $(u_n)_{n \in \N}$ and $(v_m)_{m \in \N}$.
\end{proof}


\begin{definition}[Step \ref{step:integral-of-nonnegative-measurable-function}]\label{def:integral-of-nonnegative-measurable-function}
Let $f : \Omega \to [0,\infty]$ be measurable and let $(f_n)_{n \in \N}$ be a monotonically increasing sequence of non-negative simple functions with $f = \sup_{n \in \N}f_n$.
We define
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
\int f\,\dif\mu
\coloneq
\sup_{n \in \N}
\int f_n\,\dif\mu
\end{verticalhack}
\qedhere
\]
\end{definition}

The properties from Step \ref{step:integral-of-nonnegative-simple-function} directly transfer to Step \ref{step:integral-of-nonnegative-measurable-function}.

\begin{lemma}\label{lem:elementary-properties-integral-nonnegative-measurable-functions}
Let $f,g : \Omega \to [0,\infty]$ be measurable and let $a \in [0,\infty)$.
There holds
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item $\int (f+g)\,\dif\mu = \int f\,\dif\mu + \int g\,\dif\mu$
\item $\int (af)\,\dif\mu = a\int f\,\dif\mu$
\item $f \leq g$ implies $\int f\,\dif\mu \leq \int g\,\dif\mu$
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}

By splitting a given measurable function $f : \Omega \to [-\infty,\infty]$ into its positive part
\[
f^+ \coloneq \sup\{f,0\}
\]
and its negative part
\[
f^- \coloneq \sup\{-f,0\}
\]
we arrive at the final step.

\begin{definition}[Step \ref{step:integral-of-integrable-function}]\label{def:integral-of-integrable-function}
A measurable function $f : \Omega \to [-\infty,\infty]$ is called \hl[2]{$\mu$-integrable} if
\[
\int |f|\,\dif\mu
=
\int f^+\,\dif\mu
+
\int f^-\,\dif\mu
<
\infty
\]
If either of the terms on the right hand side is bounded, we define the $\mu$-integral of $f$ according to
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
\int f\,\dif\mu
\coloneq
\int f^+\,\dif\mu
-
\int f^-\,\dif\mu
\in [-\infty,\infty]
\end{verticalhack}
\]
Finally, for $A \in \mathcal{F}$, we define the integral over $A$ by
\[
\begin{verticalhack}
\int_A f\,\dif\mu
\coloneq
\int f\ind_A\,\dif\mu
\end{verticalhack}
\qedhere
\]
\end{definition}

%   2 \Omega
% \]
% \[
%   E = m \cdot c^2
% \]

% \begin{exercise}[Borel $\sigma$-algebra on $\R$]
%   Let $\mathcal{A}_1 = \{(-\infty,a] : a \in \R\}$ and $\mathcal{A}_2 = \{(-\infty,a) : a \in \R\}$.
%   Show that $\sigma(\mathcal{A}_1) = \sigma(\mathcal{A}_2)$.
% \end{exercise}

% \begin{solution}
%   We show both directions of inclusion.

%   \emph{Step 1: Show $\mathcal{A}_2 \subset \sigma(\mathcal{A}_1)$.}

%   Fix $a \in \R$. Note that
%   \[
%     (-\infty,a) = \bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} \Bigl(-\infty,a - \tfrac{1}{n}\Bigr].
%   \]
%   Each $(-\infty,a - \tfrac{1}{n}] \in \mathcal{A}_1 \subset \sigma(\mathcal{A}_1)$, and $\sigma$-algebras are closed under countable unions, so the union is in $\sigma(\mathcal{A}_1)$. Hence $(-\infty,a) \in \sigma(\mathcal{A}_1)$.

%   Therefore $\mathcal{A}_2 \subset \sigma(\mathcal{A}_1)$, which implies $\sigma(\mathcal{A}_2) \subset \sigma(\mathcal{A}_1)$ by minimality.

%   \emph{Step 2: Show $\mathcal{A}_1 \subset \sigma(\mathcal{A}_2)$.}

%   Fix $a \in \R$. Note that
%   \[
%     (-\infty,a] = \bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} \Bigl(-\infty,a + \tfrac{1}{n}\Bigr).
%   \]
%   Each $(-\infty,a + \tfrac{1}{n}) \in \mathcal{A}_2 \subset \sigma(\mathcal{A}_2)$. Since $\sigma$-algebras are closed under countable intersections (via De Morgan: $\bigcap E_n = \bigl(\bigcup E_n^\complement\bigr)^{\complement}$), the intersection is in $\sigma(\mathcal{A}_2)$.

%   Therefore $\mathcal{A}_1 \subset \sigma(\mathcal{A}_2)$, which implies $\sigma(\mathcal{A}_1) \subset \sigma(\mathcal{A}_2)$ by minimality.

%   \emph{Conclusion:} From both inclusions, $\sigma(\mathcal{A}_1) = \sigma(\mathcal{A}_2)$.
% \end{solution}
